```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Tue, 31 Mar 2009 12:11:40 -0400, SkippyPB
> <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
>> reason of his brightness (This is in Ezekiel 28:17)

"He corrupted the wisdom by reason of his brightness"

That sounded so appealing to me, I went and looked it up.

It has nothing to do with Satan; it is a vision received by Ezekiel against 
the Prince of Tyrus, who had done very well commercially and was getting a 
bit above himself. I was disappointed. It is just the usual reminder from 
God, through "Holy Men" that if you aspire too high you'll get your 
comeuppance. Apparently, God's not too pleased by outstanding success...

Some ardent Christians believe that the Prince of Tyre was Satan in Human 
form and God sent Ezekiel to admonish him and remind him of what was in 
store for him. The claim is that because this Prince had "been in Eden, the 
Garden of God" (Ez. 18:13) it must be referring to Satan.

http://abrieflookattomorrow.com/alias.htm

I don't think so. At the time this was written (around 588 BCE) the location 
of Eden was probably pretty much general knowledge (today it is believed to 
have been around 100 miles northwest of Basra; this would put it well within 
reach of a wealthy Prince living in Tyre. (660 miles as measured on Google 
Earth)) and it would be as real a location as Mesopotamia or Italy. The area 
is famous for production of gold and precious stones.

http://www.asa3.org/ASA/PSCF/2000/PSCF3-00Hill.html

At the time Ezekiel was written, two of the four rivers mentioned in Genesis 
would have dried up, so it is unlikely to have still been a garden, but for 
people interested in gold and precious stones (and with the wealth and power 
to indulge their interest) it may well have been a popular venue.

It is quite likely that Ezekiel was simply stating a fact and that the 
prince of Tyre (a fabulously wealthy nation at the time) had, indeed, been 
to Eden.

I believe these verses say more about Ezekiel's envy than about the 
"wickedness" of the Prince of Tyre.

(Especially Ez 28 : 3 - 5) To me, there is is an unmistakeable undercurrent 
of envy running through these verses.

Coming back to...

"He corrupted the wisdom by reason of his brightness"

Throughout history "bright" people have been questioning the dogma of 
religious teaching and getting tortured and burned for doing so.. It's about 
time we all grew up. If something is truly wise it will not be "corrupted" 
by people questioning it. And all of us, irrespective of our faith (or 
lackof it) should be mature enough to practice tolerance. "I may not agree 
with your belief, but I shall defend to the death your right to hold it."

>>
>> "How art thou fallen from heaven, O Lucifer, son of the morning! how
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>> There are many more references in the Bible.

Depends WHICH Bible you read.

I noticed some minor differences between what was quoted here and what is in 
the volume I have (King James' Version published 1904).

Pete.
```

While it has been a long time since I read ANY version of the story (and 
there are many) I seem to recall that one half of the apple was poisoned and 
the other half was fine. The disguised witch took a bite from the "good" 
side to show it was fine and Snow White then ate from the poisoned side.

I don't think it requires Simple (or any other kind of) Logic to recognise 
there can be more than one version of the story. I DO recall that the 
poisoned piece lodged in her throat and so on...

My point was that this story (at least in my World) is just as relevant as 
the Pentateuch.
>
> These being pre-Heimlich-manuever days the dwarves placed her in a
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>
That joke pre-dates Benny Hill by decades. I remember hearing it in the 
playground at a school in Wellington in the 1950s:

"Hear about the bloke who poisoned his wife with a razor blade?"
"No."
"He gave her arse a nick."

(To a 10 year old, this is extremely amusing. Part of Benny Hill's appeal 
(and I am a fan of his) was because he aimed at this market...)

It is amazing how these things spread.

Pete.

```
tlmfru wrote:
> Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
> news:qqb4t41ruqu66o6ffj22dgsjqk9mi9tivv@4ax.com...
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> (How's that?  Thre generalizations in one paragraph!)

Generalizations are USUALLY logically invalid, or indeterminate, (not 
necessarily "wrong") because they imply the use of ALL. This makes them 
"sweeping statements" and it is often impossible to determine the logical 
validity of a sweeping statement.

"Islands are surrounded by water." (implicitly: "ALL islands are surrounded 
by water") is a sweeping statement  that happens to be true. (It is true 
because the DEFINITION of an island is "a body of land surrounded by 
water").

"Donkeys eat carrots." (implicitly: "ALL donkeys eat carrots") is a sweeping 
statement whose truth we simply cannot establish. If there is ONE single 
donkey somewhere on Earth that DOESN'T eat carrots then it cannot be true. 
As we are not going to check every single donkey on the planet, the logical 
truth value of the statement simply cannot be established. (Importantly, any 
further statements derived from it or based on it are also unable to be 
resolved so the whole argument can never "succeed"). However, as MOST 
donkeys eat carrots it is not WRONG. In strict logical arenas it is 
important to qualify sweeping statements with SOME, MOST, ALL, A FEW... and 
so on.

For these reasons, generalizations are (usually) best avoided. :-)

Pete.
```

Yes, in everday life that is right. But in Logic it is simply indeterminate. 
The two valued system of Aristotle has been superseded by quantum theory 
(where there are only probabilities; no actual TRUE or FALSE until the 
probability wave collapses into "Reality" (Whatever the Hell THAT is... 
:-)). A more tangible example would be "Engineering Logic" where things must 
be TRUE within tolerances. However we choose to look at it, the fact is that 
a modern Logic system has more than two values. (At the very least: TRUE, 
FALSE, and MAYBE...) For everyday communication and spoken language we don't 
really need logical rigidity and it is pedantic to be absolutely accurate 
about everything (even if we could be...) so we apply laxer rules.

It's a bit like thinking in English, when programming in COBOL... :-)


 I don't
> think it is a valid inference without context.    I'm sure that you

difference between what was intended and what was understood.

My point was that the rules I outlined apply to formal Logic and not 
(necessarily) to everyday speech.

Perhaps you are right and, because of my background with this, I have let 
the formal stuff filter through to my conversation; I really don't know. I 
DO know that I generally use qualifiers when I make generalizations :-)

Pete.

- **References:** `<72t6ovFrv73bU1@mid.individual.net> <gqnftg$pk7$1@news.dmz.bit.nl> <FJqdncODWLzfWU3UnZ2dnUVZ_oDinZ2d@earthlink.com> <gqr746$mmh$1@news.dmz.bit.nl> <gqr7g3$110$1@reader1.panix.com> <qqb4t41ruqu66o6ffj22dgsjqk9mi9tivv@4ax.com> <l3sAl.123624$2O4.115000@newsfe03.iad> <73g0eaFuipi8U1@mid.individual.net> <rpp6t45p27r64iiuktmdcao18hq6gf9fg2@4ax.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Wed, 1 Apr 2009 15:04:58 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> am not implying "ALL donkeys eat carrots".

Logically, yes you are.

That's why it requires qualification.
>
> Sometimes it doesn't matter.   "Wash your hands, because germs cause
> disease."    We don't care what percentage of germs on your hands are
> likely to cause disease.

A perfect example of the difference between common idiom and logical 
rigidity.

You can illustrate it easily with Venn diagrams. Draw a set representing the 
universe of donkeys (or germs). This is ALL donkeys or germs. Now, you can 
quite easily draw a subset that is NOT ALL but in mathematical notation you 
MUST represent it as a SUBSET.

To refer to this subset as if it was the universal set is simply wrong. 
(Logically...In spoken language we do it all the time and people understand 
what we mean.)

>
> On the other hand, killing all of the bacteria in our intestines does
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> - with some possible big upsides.

You should definitely read Kurzweil's "The Singularity is near"...

He shows that this is already happening with regard to not just alien cells 
but implanted manufactured technology. It isn't Science Fiction, he has full 
references, cites, and annotation of what is happening currently in various 
university laboratories. He believes it will produce a new "singular" 
organism by 2027. Nanotechnology, Robotics, and Computing (cybertech) are 
the three "prongs" to the trident which he believes will accomplish this. On 
the surface, it sounds unlikely, but when you read it he makes a compelling 
case. Cellular chemistry can be manipulated by nanobots to produce an 
improved organism (blood is no longer necessary, for example). I doubt that 
I'll be around to see it, or, if I am I probably won't be worried aboout 
it... Still, it is interesting reading, and some of the computer programming 
is fascinating.

Pete.
```

Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:73g0eaFuipi8U1@mid.individual.net...
> tlmfru wrote:

> >
> > I'd say that it's possible to compose any real-world generalization
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> "Islands are surrounded by water." (implicitly: "ALL islands are
surrounded
> by water") is a sweeping statement  that happens to be true. (It is true
> because the DEFINITION of an island is "a body of land surrounded by
> water").
>
> "Donkeys eat carrots." (implicitly: "ALL donkeys eat carrots") is a
sweeping
> statement whose truth we simply cannot establish. If there is ONE single
> donkey somewhere on Earth that DOESN'T eat carrots then it cannot be true.
> As we are not going to check every single donkey on the planet, the
logical
> truth value of the statement simply cannot be established. (Importantly,
any
> further statements derived from it or based on it are also unable to be
> resolved so the whole argument can never "succeed"). However, as MOST
> donkeys eat carrots it is not WRONG. In strict logical arenas it is
> important to qualify sweeping statements with SOME, MOST, ALL, A FEW...
and
> so on.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> --


Then, sir, you'll have to change your employment of them.  I quote: "People
without the benefit of COBOL experience pick it up very quickly".  ("It"
being interrupt-driven programming.  Posted January 9).  By your argument
above, ALL people without... is implied.  Therefore the statement is
logically invalid, or indeterminate.  (My objection to it, calling it a "bit
of a canard", was more to the logical inverse: that people WITH the benefit
of COBOL experience do not pick it up very quickly - because of the implied
ALL).

There's nothing in the dictionary definition of "generalize" that forbids
removing the implication of "ALL" - i.e., a statement can still be a
generalization if it includes qualifiers.  That's what I'm getting at above.

If "generalization" must imply ALL, then I guess we need another word for
those that don't

PL.
```

```
tlmfru wrote:
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:73iku7Fv9l3eU1@mid.individual.net...
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
> wonder about the limited, hermetic places they worked in.

I have worked on dozens of sites -  I did some thinking and checked my CV(35 
pages); the number I have documented is 38, but there were others where I 
was sent to fix something or solve a specific problem and these I haven't 
mentioned. My experience as a contractor was mainly in Europe and 
Australasia and the sites I worked on certainly weren't all limited or 
hermetic. (But I didn't have to train people in interrupt-driven programming 
and OO on all of them either.)

I think the problem I was trying to illustrate is that COBOL is so easily 
assimilated it is easy to believe (knowingly or unknowingly) that it is the 
ONLY way, and to fall into "COBOL think". (There is nothing wrong with 
having "COBOL think" on sites where COBOL is the only language and your job 
is to maintain and write COBOL. The problem arises when you attempt to 
expand your horizons without letting go of the concepts already ingrained in 
you.) COBOL can be a hindrance if you are unable to clear your mind (and 
most of us, when faced with new things to learn, desperately try to relate 
them to things we already know...)

For myself, I struggled with OO COBOL and made little progress for a number 
of weeks. In the end I decided to pack it in and learn Java. The same OO 
concepts which I had great difficulty with in COBOL just fell into place 
with Java. Partly it was because I approached learning Java as if I had 
never programmed before and cleared my mind of previous ideas about how 
things should work. And partly it was because Cadenhead's book is so well 
written. Anyway, after churning out some useful Java and getting into beans 
(lights started coming on about components and encapsulation), I went back 
to OO COBOL and it all made sense. Now, that is just one man's experience 
and everybody learns differently, but it taught me a few things about 
learning (and teaching) as well as how to program in Java.

> As I said
> I've done interrupt-driven programming in Cobol myself (begging your
> indulgence as we seem to have a difference of opinion here) and
> tutored a number of others, so I'd have thought that we weren't that
> unusual.

My main point was that COBOL teaches us to control things; interrupt driven 
programming teaches us to respond. My experience has been that people with 
no previous programming experience generally deal with these ideas better 
than people who only use procedural COBOL.
>
>
> Moving right along : one of your later posts on your travelling plans
> makes me wonder: ever run into a chap named Nicholas Zvegintzov?

No, I think I'd remember such an unusual name.(My travel plans are on hold 
for the time being)

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: Re: XP good until 2014 according to Microsoft

_(thread depth: 16)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2009-04-02T08:36:12-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8bi9t4p7gioighcuhqco9vnpplsqtg5ujb@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<72t6ovFrv73bU1@mid.individual.net> <gqnftg$pk7$1@news.dmz.bit.nl> <FJqdncODWLzfWU3UnZ2dnUVZ_oDinZ2d@earthlink.com> <gqr746$mmh$1@news.dmz.bit.nl> <gqr7g3$110$1@reader1.panix.com> <qqb4t41ruqu66o6ffj22dgsjqk9mi9tivv@4ax.com> <l3sAl.123624$2O4.115000@newsfe03.iad> <73g0eaFuipi8U1@mid.individual.net> <VkRAl.131711$RJ7.123990@newsfe18.iad>`

```
On Wed, 1 Apr 2009 16:49:44 -0600, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:

>If "generalization" must imply ALL, then I guess we need another word for
>those that don't

My contention is that generalization does not always imply ALL.   We
can and do communicate quite effectively with generalizations that
are, more general.

Programmers are used to absolute definitions, and we like logic that
works with absolutes.   But the world isn't absolute, and certainly
human languages aren't absolute.     It works quite nicely to say
"children are smaller than adults", without someone showing up with a
small adult and a large child to "disprove" the generalization.

My undergraduate major was physics.  It was very, very hard for
physicists to accept that physics wasn't as absolute as they were
comfortable with a century ago.   But other disciplines have accepted
fuzziness as being part of their reality.
```

> In the movie _The Four Musketeers_, D'Artagnan has a ticket or pass to
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> a tragedy than if a kid gets kidnapped down the street.

I was in Bangla Desh and saw bodies left by receding floodwaters just lying 
by the roadside. For me, it is every bit as much a tragedy as a local 
kidnapping.. But, I take your point.
>
> Oh - last summer, we took a family of Kiwis on some sight-seeing (they
> were here in Denver for last year's World Science Fiction Convention).

Kiwis love to travel. It is probably because we grow up in such an isolated 
place. Most of us believe (especially when young) that the world must be 
much better than where we are (more excitement, sexier technology, prettier 
sexual partners, etc.) As a result it is normal for young people here to do 
at least one "OE" (Overseas Experience), usually a working holiday to the UK 
(and latterly the USA, both countries providing reciprocal working visas so 
their young people can tour and work here), with accompanying holiday for 
sightseeing. Some of us, (like me), hold dual passports so we can live and 
work in Europe or Australasia. New Zealand does not limit dual nationality 
and, although I am something of an Anglophile, my first loyalty is to the 
place Iwas born (NZ). That has never been changed by having more than one 
nationality.

I'm sure you all enjoyed the time, Howard. The Kiwis would treasure the 
memories of Denver, Colorado and will tell everyone at home about the 
hospitality they encountered there :-)

> The dad and son laughed at noticing that the partial moon was facing
> the wrong way.    It took me some thinking to figure out why this
> would appear to be so (they probably only notice it in the evening).

I remember passing the time on a long haul flight once doing drawings on a 
napkin to help me conceptualize why Orion is "upside down" in the Northern 
Hemisphere... :-)

[Before we get a deluge of mail protesting that it is the other way round, 
consider this: In the North his dog (Sirius) runs ahead of him, which no 
well trained hunting dog would do; in the South Sirus walks to heel, 
awaiting his masters command. And before you say he is standing on his head 
in the South, we have a different construction for the constellation, which 
is just as plausible as yours, and puts him on his feet. :-)]

I figured that at the Equator, he should be lying down. We got to Singapore 
(which is almost on the Equator) and I was delighted to find my prediction 
was accurate :-)

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Orthogonality in Cobol

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-04-02T14:06:47+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<73ihd8Fvck0dU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<03cb6d2a-46e9-4f71-b28f-442afad3e94c@s20g2000yqh.googlegroups.com>`

```
Anthony Aquino wrote:
> Hi guys...
>
> Can you give me an example of orthogonality in cobol, please?

A component, written in COBOL, which has a stable interface. There is an 
example on the new Cobdata web site which will be published shortly. (The 
site is almost ready but my ISP decided to upgrade to Server 2008 and the 
result is custard... it should be sorted within a week.)

> Or cobol
> isnt orthogonal?

Procedural COBOL CAN be orthogonal (modular programming is a step in the 
right direction, but it doesn't guarantee orthogonality), although in 
practice (certainly from the procedural code I've seen) it usually isn't. 
This leads to much wailing and gnashing of teeth on many COBOL sites when a 
few lines of code get changed, and something in a subsystem far away falls 
apart.

Object Oriented COBOL, when written using component based techniques, is 
DEFINITELY orthogonal. Like other OO languages the concept of 
"Encapsulation" ensures that it is.

>
> I think orthogonality is a complex concept...

Like most complex things, they can be conceptualized in ways that make them 
less complex. Imagine an isolation ward in a hospital. All access to the 
patients is carefully controlled and the staff cannot remove items from the 
ward and use them elsewhere.

The mathematical, and lower level computer language meanings of 
orthogonality are a bit more confusing (until you actually study them (then 
they're not)) but the above would serve as a general example.
>
> Thanks.
HTH,

Pete.
```

some COBOL in which any instruction can use any register in nay addressing 
mode... did I get that right?' then it is another matter, entire, and 
deserves a different response.

Thanks for the link Kellie.  In the article it states, "The term has
been extended to general use, meaning the characteristic of being
independent (relative to something else). It also can mean:
non-redundant, non-overlapping, or irrelevant. In computer
terminology, something - such as a programming language or a data
object - is orthogonal if it can be used without consideration as to
how its use will affect something else."

I want to know who "extended" its meaning to be something so obtuse?

I can't see a connection to orthogonality here. These are two elements of 
the same entity. TOGETHER they provide a facility. Were you referring to 
orthogonal persistence of data? Sorry, if I'm missing something here.
>
> Usage vs PIC?
>

Again, there is no conflict between usage and the picture clause. Things 
with pictures still have usage (explicity or implicitly). If you are talking 
about SIZE, CLASS, and USAGE   (which is how we defined data before PICTURE 
was invented), then there could be orthogonality inasmuch as programs which 
used PICTURE should not be affected when accessing data items defined with 
SIZE,CLASS, and USAGE. (It is a clumsily contrived example, though).

Am I missing something here, or could you clarify, please?

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Orthogonality in Cobol

- **From:** e p chandler <epc8@juno.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-02T17:34:53-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bda998e8-101b-4036-8535-a2db7088d1a6@q9g2000yqc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<03cb6d2a-46e9-4f71-b28f-442afad3e94c@s20g2000yqh.googlegroups.com> <a2fcf816-814e-42f2-b5f8-35f1ca1308d6@v15g2000yqn.googlegroups.com> <73krvnFvfcs7U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Apr 2, 6:19 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> e p chandler wrote:
> > On Apr 1, 12:11 pm, Anthony Aquino <anthonyjeffer...@gmail.com> wrote:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> orthogonal persistence of data? Sorry, if I'm missing something here.

[snip]

> Am I missing something here, or could you clarify, please?

I'm talking more about how free are you to combine sets of different
things?

Look at DD's example. He says "Any instruction can use any register in
any addressing mode." For some instruction sets that is probably true.
On the 8088 you can not use AX as an index register. So the 8088's
instruction set is not orthogonal in that respect.

Likewise there is an interaction between a file's organization and how
it can be accessed. Perhaps this is tautological? However there is
SOME freedom to handle "random access" files sequentially as well. So
you have SOME flexibility.

But COBOL is not completely rigid. Look at MOVE. Move is quite
flexible. It's actually polymorphic. Arithmetic operations also work
with a variety of data types. Constrast that with languages in which
you have to invoke a method on an object to get it to convert itself
to a different data type.....

Then there are languages like "C" in which you can combine almost
anything with anything else. It will almost certainly compile, it
might even do something that you want, or it might start WWIII. In
that sense "C" is more orthogonal. You have more expressive power with
which you can shoot yourself in the foot!

I hope this makes SOME sense. [It did when I posted it. :-)]

If you want to combine COMPONENTS almost at will, as you describe,
then they are orthogonal too!

--e
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Orthogonality in Cobol

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

```
e p chandler wrote:
> On Apr 2, 6:19 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> any addressing mode."

That should be qualified to "...in any addressing mode supported by that 
register." (He did say he wasn't sure... :-))

> For some instruction sets that is probably true.
> On the 8088 you can not use AX as an index register. So the 8088's
> instruction set is not orthogonal in that respect.

I don't think so :-)

Orthogonality at this level is more about the ability to save and restore an 
environment (context), so that a process can be interrupted and resumed 
later, without suffering because its registers were re-used in the interim. 
If it CAN'T do that then it is not an orthogonal architecture. How it 
actually accomplishes it (hardware, middleware, or software) is not really 
important; if processes can run in isolation then they are orthogonal.

>
> Likewise there is an interaction between a file's organization and how
> it can be accessed. Perhaps this is tautological? However there is
> SOME freedom to handle "random access" files sequentially as well. So
> you have SOME flexibility.

OK, I see what you are saying. Thanks.
>
> But COBOL is not completely rigid. Look at MOVE. Move is quite
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> I hope this makes SOME sense. [It did when I posted it. :-)]

Yes, the clarification helps although I'm not totally convinced by some of 
it :-) It isn't important enough to argue about.

>
> If you want to combine COMPONENTS almost at will, as you describe,
> then they are orthogonal too!

Er... I rather hoped that was my point... :-)

Pete.
```

It sounds like program orthogonality is kind of like "object", or
"database".    It could mean almost anything until some organization
specifies criteria and the programming community accepts those
criteria.

Then, when there is a demand for the next level of independence, a new
word is co opted.

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:73krvnFvfcs7U1@mid.individual.net...
…[42 more quoted lines elided]…
> but I probably would choose the word 'inconsistent' instead.

(Good to see you are still here, Charlie... :-))

I think your observation is very well spotted.

"inconsistency" does it for me (and I said so at the time...)

It would be "unorthogonal" if you COULD use AFTER in an inline PERFORM, but 
the result you obtained was DIFFERENT from using it out of line.

The fact that you can't do it doesn't mean you won't get the same results 
from what you CAN do, both in and out of line with PERFORM. So it is 
orthogonal, but definitely inconsistent in the way the construct is applied.

Pete.
```

Charles Hottel wrote:
<snip>>
> Yes I am still here, but I have made a conscious choice to limit what
> I post.  I find it is very easy to be instantly misunderstood and to
> get attacked without any attempts to ask for clarification.

That is pretty usual on Usenet. I believe this group is one of the better 
ones. Certainly, over the years, it has become less abrasive, but it is 
inevitable that where bright people, who feel passionately about something, 
disagree, heat is generated.

I can't speak for others, but posting here regularly has been good for my 
own growth. I value the people who post here, even if and when I disagree 
with them, and that respect usually prevents me from descending into 
flamethrowing. (Sometimes I can't help it, but I like to think I'm getting 
better at seeing what is REALLY important as I get older...)

The important thing is whether things are done in a fit of temper or whether 
there is real malice behind it.

Fortunately, there are very few occasions when there is malice or spite 
behind what is said here.

Meanness is rare, but occasionally, because people are Human, their emotions 
get the better of them.

All of us yell at our loved ones occasionally; we don't love them any the 
less because of it.

I can't honestly say I've never regretted anything I posted here, but it has 
been a long time since I did.

As far as you're concerned Charlie, I've always enjoyed your posts here. You 
put me onto Kurzweil and I enjoyed his book immensely and it stimulated 
other ideas for me.

>Perhaps
> what I post is not expressed clearly enough, but I think part of it
> is that many people are instant critics and I don't what to waste my
> time defending myself.

That is a fair statement, based on the evidence. I don't see a problem with 
you expressing yourself. Your thoughts are quite clear to me. It IS natural 
for people to want to question stuff (especially when you are dealing with 
people who have keen minds).

I'm not sure that defending yourself is a "waste of time"... It certainly is 
if no minds are going to be changed :-) but sometimes, even without 
necessarily changing someone's mind, something that is said can stimulate 
thought in other directions, and defending a position through civilzed 
"argument" (in the classical sense, not the pub bar sense, where the 
loudest, or biggest, meanest guy is usually the "rightest"...:-)), can cause 
you to re-evaluate your own position. Even if you don't change it, at least 
you become aware of what may or may not be weaknesses in it.

It is also fair to say that there isn't much point in posting something 
UNLESS you really believe it and are prepared to defend it.

Having said all of that, it can become tiresome defendng somethng where you 
have already explained your position at length. Sometimes there is nothing 
more you want to say about it. (I call this Gingerbread Man syndrome, 
because, after the fox snapped his head off "...the little Ginger Bread Man 
had nothing more to say.". ) Arguing just for the sake of it, IS a wasteof 
time.

>Part of it is also my problem as I can be too
> thin skinned.

Despite what you may think, I don't have the skin of a rhino and there have 
been many times when I have been wounded by exchanges here. But this is what 
I meant when I spoke about "growth".  What doesn't kill us makes us 
stronger. I get less wounded nowadays, partly because most of the exchanges 
here are more civilized (I guess all of us are getting older and, maybe, 
wiser), partly because I realise it is just foolish to get upset about what 
people say on Usenet, partly because the interactions here (and in life in 
general) have helped me to realise that most people have something to say 
and more often than not, it is worth listening to and considering, before 
responding. I liked Doc's statement that if he wanted agreement he'd talk to 
a mirror... It is worth remembering.

If you look at the topics that get covered here it is way beyond COBOL.  Do 
you think the Newsgroup is any less valuable because of that? I don't. I 
think the fact that it is a lively forum is fantastic. I know it is tiresome 
for some people who ONLY want to discuss COBOL, but, provided topics are 
clearly marked as OT, I can't see any harm in it. In fact, I have found the 
OT discussions to be much more stimulating than the strictly technical ones.

We NEED both kinds of discussion to make it worth visiting here.

> This is not directed at you, it is just a general
> comment.

Thank you for letting me off the hook... :-) (Not sure I deserve it, but 
appreciated... :-))
>
> Today I was watching a video of Donald Knuth giving a talk about his
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> varying instruction formats.  He even used the word "consistency".

Excellent!

The very first machine I ever programmed (NCR 500) had a fixed format 
instruction set and it was a 4 address system. (Do this with this, put the 
result here, then go here for your next instruction. In effect, EVERY 
instruction was a "Do something and branch..."  (Maybe that's why I was much 
less sensitive than many people when I encountered ALTER in COBOL a couple 
of years later; modifying instructions on the run was second nature to me 
:-))) That machine had 4.8K and could perform just over 100,000 IPS (.1 
MIPS), but the inherent power of the 4 address system enabled it to do many 
things which, on reflection, were quite remarkable. It could maintain 
accounts, produce invoices, and I even wrote a sort for it.

Just in closing...

I would urge ANYONE who enjoys browsing here to post as well, if you don't 
already. Think through what you feel about something, organise your thoughts 
on it, then state them. What's the worst that can happen? HeyBub says your 
cat could get warts from Open Software,  but no-one here has suggested any 
dire personal consequences from posting.

Don't be intimidated by the "universality" of the forum, but do remember 
that what you say here may be relayed and can turn up in the most unexpected 
places. (I have been quite stunned to see stuff I wrote years ago still 
being debated in forums I never heard of or subscribed to... :-))

The Internet is indeed a wondrous place; be a part of it :-)

Pete.

so they may have been withdrawn or replaced. I found his website and there 
are free videos, but not the 16 videos which I got for free. A quick search 
on MSDN revealed about 20,000 sites offering learning materials for C#, many 
of which are free.

I would (and did...) start at the "Absolute Beginner" level and pretend you 
know nothing. You can learn at your own pace and I was productive very 
quickly. (First serious C# application code after 2 weeks) In the last 18 
months or so I have grown to really enjoy C# programming and the amazing 
things you can do with it, but, it is just another programming language... 
:-)

I looked at some of the links the MSDN search turned up and I really liked 
this one... enjoy the video (run it full screen for best results)!

http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-nz/beginner/bb308730(en-us).aspx

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Orthogonality in Cobol

- **From:** bo774@FreeNet.Carleton.CA (Kelly Bert Manning)
- **Date:** 2009-04-12T22:20:31+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<grtpff$pv7$1@theodyn.ncf.ca>`
- **References:** `<03cb6d2a-46e9-4f71-b28f-442afad3e94c@s20g2000yqh.googlegroups.com>`

```
Anthony Aquino (anthonyjefferson@gmail.com) writes:
> Hi guys...
> 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Thanks.

How about 
	ADD GIN TO VERMOUTH GIVING MARTINI
and 
	COMPUTE MARTINI = VERMOUTH + GIN
demonstrating orthogonality in COBOL?

Depends on what you mean by orthogonality.

The only time I have ever seen it used in connection with COBOL was in a
comparison with PL/I.

COBOL was described as being more orthogonal than PL/I because there is 
often only one way to do something.

In PL/I there are often multiple ways to achieve the same result. A few work
well, most are in the middle, and others are ones to avoid like the plague.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Text windows size under XP

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Rene_Surop <infodynamics_ph@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-03T01:36:19-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<e7696450-31a4-4bcc-bbf1-23e62f825698@b7g2000pre.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<8822871f-1e25-4e5e-a40d-470c4cddc6ed@k19g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <wPSdnTlx1KqKMEnUnZ2dnUVZ_gyWnZ2d@earthlink.com> <004ef1a0-4792-45e7-a275-3303b59bf643@s22g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <ee77b825-7b19-4af5-bba3-46ebf16f2a25@s1g2000prd.googlegroups.com>`

```
> So is the bug in my program or in Net Express?
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> But then what would I know about this, it's on Windows

Micro Focus requires 'Application Server' for you to execute an .EXE
file. If you setup (configure) your application server to.. say c:
\mfapps then you have to copy your files (.EXE/.DLLs) to that folder
for you to run it independently. Application Server is a separate
setup and this is the one that provides the RUNTIME apps.

However, you CANNOT (i think) install Application Server if you have
NetExpress 5.2 Personal Edition installed in the same PC. In this
case, you can run your application only from 'within' the NetExpress
IDE.

Personal Edition as discussed only limits up to 2200 lines of codes
(but not memory limitations). You can (if permitted) chop down your
codes in less than 2000 lines and make the others as DLL. Better yet,
make them components so that you can use its full potential.

Keep supporting MF. For us to be viable.... :)
```

Rene: I have always been aware of the 2200 lines limit (see my earlier
posting.)

However, Net Express Personal has an option to install Enterprise
Server (all 52mb of it) described as a "scalable, managed and high
performance transactional environment for the deployment of COBOL
applications ..." Why offer this option if standalone .EXE files
cannot be run independently of the IDE?

Further, one of the Build options for a project is a Generic Release
build for which .OBJ files are shown as output in the Project Browser.
If one then looks at the Link Wizard in Project Build settings one of
the questions asks whether a standalone .EXE file is required. This
would appear to suggest that programs can be run independently of the
IDE.

So can anyone help me further with this?

Alternately has anybody managed to get Microsoft (Microfocus) Cobol5
compiling correctly under XP? (!)

> On Apr 5, 5:17 pm, rip...@Azonic.co.nz wrote:
>
…[112 more quoted lines elided]…
> and ADISCTRL. As I understand it, ADIS is the main run-time support

ADISCF is the configuration program.

> module for Screen Section and enhanced ACCEPT/DISPLAY and doesn't
> exist as a separate program.

In ../LBR there are ADIS.OBJ, ADISINT.OBJ and ADISKEY.OBJ. Either
statically link these into your .EXE or build a callable module with
them that is on your COBDIR, as explained in the User Guide Ch.3.
```

```
On Apr 6, 11:41 am, paolorica...@gmail.com wrote:
> On Apr 6, 4:53 am, rip...@Azonic.co.nz wrote:
>
…[148 more quoted lines elided]…
> yearssince I did any of this stuff and my memory must be failing!)

The ADISxxx.OBJ are OBJECTs and NOT libraries, thus they get linked in
the OBJ parameter of the LINK command:

From page 19 of the User Guide:

LINK MYPROG+ADIS+ADISINIT+ADISKEY+EXTFH,,,COBLIB+COBAPI;

You can actually link the ADIS and EXTFH objects to be callable
modules.

LINK ADIS+ADISINIT+ADISKEY,,,COBLIB+COBAPI;
LINK EXTFH,,,COBLIB+COBAPI;

and put these in the COBDIR= to be loaded dynamically.
```

```
paoloricardo@gmail.com wrote:
> On Apr 6, 11:52 am, rip...@Azonic.co.nz wrote:
> 
>>On Apr 6, 1:16 pm, paolorica...@gmail.com wrote:

>>>and what would I put in the COBDIR variable? (Sorry I don't have the
>>>User Guide documentation you refer to)
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

> OK, well thanks for your help so far.

A little paraphrasing - 'He said" and 'I said' was getting tiresomely 
long :-)

Can't help you with the books for MSCOBOL5 - chucked 'em out ages ago.
The basic ADIS module very likely hasn't changed too much over the 
years. Whether there is any difference between the MS issued version of 
ADIS and the MF version - just have to take a chance.

Have you checked out the on-line version of ADIS for Net Express V5.1; 
see :-

http://supportline.microfocus.com/documentation/books/nx51ws01/nx51indx.htm

Select the manual for Character User Interfaces.

Now I know this wont necessarily tie in with MSCOBOL; there might be 
enhancements that work only with MFCOBOL (Net Express). If some options 
you select aren't available to MSCOBOL5, then they should show up or not 
work.

If and when you get back to N/E Personal Edition DON'T use the batch 
configuration file you have already listed for use with MICROSOFT COBOL.
You can use the N/E Default; any pathnames should point at the Net 
Express software. I can tell you from doing a search today between 
MSCOBOL5/MFCOBOL3(DOS) and Net Express V3.1, that the size for the 
various ADIS files in the latter are larger. Doesn't mean the basic ADIS 
has been changed, just that Micro Focus may have packaged them differently.

I didn't mess around with ADIS but just used the defaults in N/E V 3.1.
See if you have the same fun and games with this program using Personal 
Edition V 5.2-

*>---------------------------------------------------------------

Program-id. ScreenTest1.

Working-Storage Section.
01 charx    pic x.
*> Procedure Division.

perform 60 times
     display "Hello World"
end-perform

*> Either case, Debug or Release - displays a DOS Text 'window'
*> approx 9 x 5 inches at top of screen, (80 x 25); using
*> MAXIMIZE button merely shifts the DOS Window from Left-side
*> to Right-side of screen and vice versa 


*> Generic DEBUG Build - scrolls down until it hits 60, but you
*> only see 24 lines

*> Generic RELEASE Build. Real cute; you get the DOS Window as
*> above but not one single line of 'Hello World' !

accept charx
STOP RUN.
*>---------------------------------------------------------------

I'm not bothering to repeat it, but using same Defaults above, I tested 
another program using Screen Section to draw a box, using dots/periods 
to make up the lines. Worked fine, in both DEBUG and RELEASE modes, but 
limited to top of the screen and moving left and right on MAXIMIZING as 
described above.

Using Net Express with a Win 98 machine, I was using the MS PWB Editor 
and got it to go FULL SCREEN. (That was solely for editing - not 
compile/linking, which I did in the N/E IDE). Doing the same thing with 
Windows XP - click on my PWB Icon and it goes belly up ! So I dropped 
the PWB feature and went with the Editor in the N/E IDE.

I'm not sure what your purpose is in trying to prove old DOS Text 
Display programs work with the 'latest and greatest'. If you *are* 
interested in upgrading, then as a minimum I would politely suggest you 
should be making a decision about GUI-ing  :-

1. dotNet and Basic for screening
2. Dialog Editor - GUI classes - see :-
	Program Files---->Net Express---->Base----->Demo----->Bank.
	Or this one - substituting 'GUISAMP' for 'Bank' above
	
3. Dialog System - GUI packaged apps - see :-
   Program Files--->Net Express-->Dialog System-->Demo-->CommonControls

Note that there is a file listing all methods applicable to all N/E 
classes - "C:\Program Files\Net Express\Base\BIN\MFNXCL31.CHM"). The 
methods in this file can be used with both #2 and #3 above. (Probably
reads "\MFNXCL51.CHM" in your case.)

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

paoloricardo@gmail.com wrote:

> Jimmy: as you have probably deduced from the foregoing this all
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> Paul - Melbourne, Australia

Having made the foray into C#, you are just a 'dabbler' with COBOL.

Rather than concern yourself about whether or not programs work, you 
would be better advised to communicate with Micro Focus - (1) What's the 
price for the full product ? (2) How much is annual maintenance ? (3) 
What other extras do I have to pay for, such as runtime fees ?.

Subject to answers you receive you may well as above, not persevere - 
problem solved !

I think I can confirm that you CANNOT generate standalone EXEs, but that 
doesn't mean  you can't produce them - to demonstrate to yourself with 
working executables. The FREE Academic or Personal editions are a 
marketing tool - suck it and see, and if you like it, buy the full 
product. Therefore the 'freebies' have limitations, number of lines and 
you can't distribute applications. Nothing devious in that, just a 
marketing technique, which they are upfront about.

(If you want 'devious' consider the switch from MSCOBOL5 to MFCOBOL3 - 
remember the 'glossy' included with V5 - some outfit called Micro Focus 
also had a COBOL compiler, about $1,000 - but when the 'goods' arrived 
you didn't have the full package for the $1K. You have used VISOC - good 
product, was about $CDN 800. Ah, but then they introduced something 
called Net Express - then prices started to go up - that was devious, 
and for many people Micro Focus will never live it down).

When you get into the nitty gritty my guess is that you aren't going to 
persevere.


Jimmy, Calgary AB

```
paoloricardo@gmail.com wrote:
> On Apr 7, 6:42 am, "James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletet...@shaw.ca> wrote:
>
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
>> live it down).

For many people the problem was not so much that they introduced a new 
product, rather it was that they stopped supporting the one that many people 
had already bought. There should have been an easy (and probably, free...) 
"migration path" from VisOC to NE. I don't recall that there ever was.

>>
>> When you get into the nitty gritty my guess is that you aren't going
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> am concerned.



I am amazed at the spooky similarity between Paul's exerience and my own. I 
thought the way MF (to be fair, it was a "different" MF from today's 
company) just dumped the VisOC community was appalling, and it was a major 
factor in sending me to Fujitsu.(I was a Micro Focus user at the time...)

The move to C# came when I couldn't get any response from Fujitsu, regarding 
their NetCOBOL for .NET compiler. As it turned out, it wasn't needed for 
bringing my existing apps to .NET anyway, so I guess, in retrospect, they 
did me a favour. (It certainly saved me several thousand dollars I had 
allocated for it.)

> As for communicating with MF, I've searched their site
> and can find forums, but most of the messages are several years old -
> which doesn't strike me as being characteristic of a particularly
> healthy community.

The COBOL community generally is a bit down at the moment. Many people see 
their jobs evaporating, new technologies which seem strange and foreign 
replacing old, tried, and trusted ways of doing things; it is all a bit 
bewildering. Nevertheless, there are quite a few who are making the 
transition and I see "migration" as a growth industry. I was looking at 
companies offering migration tools for COBOL (I have such a toolset, whichI 
have built over several years, and wanted to get an idea of who else is 
doing it and what they are offering) and was quite surprised by the number, 
and the quality of what they offer. There are tools to move mainframe COBOL 
to just about any platform. My own toolset is designed primarily for moving 
COBOL to the .NET platform (which is what I had to do with my own stuff), 
but I could easily amend it if there was a market.

The major parts of my toolset are written in C# (although it uses COBOL 
components). That is significant for me. Even a few years back I would not 
have considered writing critical commercial code in anything other than 
COBOL.

A long time ago, (1996ish) I suggested that COBOL would not be the language 
of choice for development by 2015. I see no need to revise that, but it 
isn't all gloom for COBOL either.

Whether we believe Gartner or not, there is a HUGE amount of COBOL code in 
existence.

It would be crazy to throw the baby out with the bath water.  I see COBOL 
being refactored and integrated with newer technology and I see that process 
extending beyond 2015.

Companies like Micro Focus and Fujitsu are pushing transition to 
client/server. The tools I have seen are excellent, but they are not cheap. 
(Mine are :-))

I wouldn't recommend a young person to make a career in COBOL programming (I 
did advise this, frequently, even 25 years ago) but that doesn't mean that 
existing COBOL people have no future. The answer is to expand the skillset. 
Learn Java. Learn C#. Get into the Web. It just needs some time and effort; 
you don't even need money. Compilers are free, toolsets are free,

In retrospect, I believe the best thing I did was move off COBOL. However, I 
still have a soft spot for the language I made a living out of for over 20 
years.
>
> Anyway, you have confirmed that NE won't produce standalone
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> present!

It would be good if you can get the old application going, but it is likely 
to be difficult without the manual(s).  I had a search around in the "really 
old stuff" I have stored but couldn't find it. It is probably in a different 
storage location  than I have here at home. I DID find a number of treasures 
like a set of Fujitsu COBOL 4 manuals, a Programming manual for the PSION 
Organiser, and some 8086 Assembler manuals... :-)

If it does happen to turn up I'll let you know. I seem to remember it was 
blue and white so I must have had a copy...

Pete.

Pete: that's exactly why I'm trying to 'resurrect' my old COBOL
programs. As I mentioned earlier, I still have the MF 3.1.54
Professional COBOL system complete with parallel port dongle and a
full set of manuals. I'm now investigating whether or not I can use
this compiler by booting into DOS from a DOS boot disk. Some have
suggested running under Win95 compatibility mode but I'm not sure
whether XP will allow access to the parallel port even in this mode.

If not, it'll be NE for debugging and MSCOBOL to create the .EXE.

While still on this nostalgia trip, I learnt COBOL using MF CIS-COBOL
on a BBC micro and a Torch Z80 second processor/disk pack. Now, it
just so happens that I've also been recently playing with a BBC
emulator and BBC disk image of the CIS-COBOL compiler. I don't know
why....!!
```

Just an update on this. Booting into DOS didn't work - only drive a:\
was recognised. Setting Win95 compatibility didn't work either.

which allows access to I/O ports under XP. So my dongle is recognised
and I have Professional COBOL 3.1.54 working on my XP PC.

```
paoloricardo@gmail.com wrote:
> On Apr 8, 9:33 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> Open COBOL. A decent Intellisensed-IDE is nowadays essential.

Yes, I can't imagine life without it. It just saves hours of work.

I started off with the Express Edition of VS 2005. I'm not positive about 
this but I'm almost sure I didn't upgrade it to the full version. Maybe I 
did.. anyway, I added a number of add-ins without problem. The best was 
SimulSoft reporting. A Client wasn't confident with Crystal and had heard 
about this Russian reporting system. I agreed to check it out and I was 
extremely impressed. It integrates seamlessly with VS and lets you prepare 
reports with a GUI drag n drop interface. You can use any RDB as a data 
source and it does all the connection strings and connects directly through 
OLEDB or you CAN connect through ODBC if you want to. Brilliant! There were 
a few other things that were more concerned with supporting development but 
everything I added installed and worked out of the box.

I decided to invest in the full version of VS 2008 and get it out of the 
money I saved on NOT buying NetCOBOL for .NET :-).

Have you had a look on e-bay or TradeMe? Sometimes people attend Microsoft 
functions and get stuff which they have no idea how to use. It ends up being 
sold on e-bay for a fraction of the full price and these are legitimate, 
licensed copies. If you have any contacts who are licensed MS developers 
they often get a batch of licenses which they are not using. While they 
can't legally sell you one, they can put you on one of them as an 
employee...

The full Editions are worth the money. If you can get them for less it is a 
real bargain.

Pete.
```

Perhaps there is another option for you.

faster under DOSEMU in Linux than directly under DOS. The same was true
for execution of the programs.

environment and that might also be true for today.

I remember it well. Therewas huge resistance to the dongle on Enterprise 
sites and this led to a version being produced (only for bulk orders of the 
software) which did not require the dongle. I believe there wrere some 
hacked versions which bypassed the dongle validation also.



>I'm now investigating whether or not I can use
> this compiler by booting into DOS from a DOS boot disk. Some have
> suggested running under Win95 compatibility mode but I'm not sure
> whether XP will allow access to the parallel port even in this mode.

There is one certain way to find out... :-)

>
> If not, it'll be NE for debugging and MSCOBOL to create the .EXE.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> why....!!

Ah, it is an addiction like heroin... :-)

I found myself writing sample COBOL code to solve a problem in a COBOL forum 
the other day. It was a young recent grad who had been thrown into COBOL and 
took a fair crack at trying to solve the problem, but his code was pretty 
terrible (as you might expect).  I look at bad COBOL and just want to fix 
it... I think there are many here who feel the same way.

Pete.

-----------message from Micro Focus World
Event-------------------------------------------------------------------------
I am sure that you may all be aware that the Micro FocusWorld Event
has now moved online, and is now Micro Focus Live. The actual physical
event at Dallas is not taking place any longer and will now be
available online as a virtual event – and completely free of charge to
attend.

This impacts the COBOL evolution contest, as the prize was supposed to
be a trip to MFW – Dallas which is no longer possible. Inlight of
these changes, we have decided to postpones the COBOL Evolution
Contest deadline.
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Something similar happened with COBOL World 2003 in Anaheim. (I had planned 
to present there).

The Organisers simply couldn't get enough support to make it financially 
viable. They were honest and up-front about it and I bear them no resentment 
whatsoever. (Like you, I put in considerable effort to preparing a 
presentation (although I didn't lose sleep over it... :-))

interest or finances available (in the current climate) for MF to consider 
this a "go".

They notified you at the last minute because they would want to wait until 
the last minute in the hope of getting more registrations.

I'm supposed to attend an "Agile Management" conference in London in 
September this year, but this time I'm not preparing anything until much 
closer to the event and, frankly, I'll be surprised if it happens... Times 
are tough. (I had planned on being en-route to the U.K. right now but I've 
delayed it because things there look even worse than here. (If I'm going to 
be poor, I'd rather be poor at home... :-)))

Look on the bright side... the work you have done might be saleable, and 
even if it isn't, you gained valuable experience by doing it.

Pete.

[posted and emailed]

In article <7c0dd106-c1e1-46bc-9342-0186f061dce6@r33g2000yqn.googlegroups.com>,
Agustin Zabala  <agustinza@gmail.com> wrote:
>Vacantes de Programadores (Vacancy)

When posting to comp.lang.cobol please include a rate, or range of rates, 
associated with the position(s) offered; doing otherwise leads many to 
conclude that you are either trolling for resumes or running a blind ad to 
determine rates.

According to babelfish.altavista.com:

--begin quoted text:

Cuando la fijacin a comp.lang.cobol incluye por favor un ndice, o la gama 
de tarifas, asociada a las posiciones ofrecidas; el hacer de otra manera 
lleva muchos a concluir que usted est pescando con cebo de cuchara para 
los curriculums vitae o el funcionamiento un anuncio oculto para 
determinar tarifas.

--end quoted text

Simply put: if you do not post how much you are willing to pay for the 
skills you are asking someone to provide you may causing the exact people 
you would most want to work with you to consider your advertisement as 
unworthy of a reply.

According to http://babelfish.altavista.com:

--begin quoted text:

Puesto simplemente: si usted no le fija cunto usted est dispuesto a pagar 
las habilidades est pidiendo que alguien le proporcionara puede causando a 
la gente exacta que usted querra ms trabajar con usted para considerar su 
anuncio como indigno de una contestacin.

--end quoted text

DD
```

## Thread: Zvegintzov — 3 msgs

### Zvegintzov

- **From:** "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net>
- **Date:** 2009-04-02T23:12:04-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<g1gBl.23$5j.19@newsfe15.iad>`

```
He founded and ran a magazine called "Software Maintenane News" in the "80's
& '90's.  He also was something of a name at all sorts of software meetings
and such like.  He sold the magazine subsciption list to a programming
manual which I don't think did very well out of the deal.  Anyway, he did
get around a lot and I thought maybe some of you might have encountered him.
I guess the world isn't that small, after all.

He was important to me as he was the first to pay me for any of my I/T
articles.  Now he's long since retired & runs a B & B out of his house on
Staten Island.  How are the mighty fallen ...

PL
```

#### ↳ Re: Zvegintzov

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-04-03T12:29:24+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gr4vf4$nt1$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<g1gBl.23$5j.19@newsfe15.iad>`

```
In article <g1gBl.23$5j.19@newsfe15.iad>, tlmfru <lacey@mts.net> wrote:
>He founded and ran a magazine called "Software Maintenane News" in the "80's
>& '90's.

[snip]

>Now he's long since retired & runs a B & B out of his house on
>Staten Island.  How are the mighty fallen ...

As long as he enjoys his work, benefits others and can manage his finances 
in a manner so as not to cause him too much worry I'd argue against use of 
words like 'fallen'... I remember, decades on back, working with 
corner-cubicle managers who made no bones about how much they disliked 
their work and were in it only for the pension so they could retire and 
get a franchise for one of those (at the time) new-fangled 
gasoline-stations with a convenience-store attached.

Compare and contrast with the career of George Melies, the 'father of the 
special-effects movie' or 'Cinemagician'.

DD
```

## Thread: Looking for the world's most gawd-awful COBOL program — 6 this month / 8 total

### Looking for the world's most gawd-awful COBOL program

- **From:** mike@introspect.com
- **Date:** 2009-04-08T18:37:13-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4239b064-fcff-48ba-a85c-7e5b3ecf9a13@k8g2000yqn.googlegroups.com>`

```
I'm looking for the absolute worst to maintain COBOL program.  It
sould include a plethora of ALTER and GO statements.  This program
should violate every best-practice of software development.  This
program should access files that are completely unnormalized.
However, this program should compile and run :-)

I need this program to test the tools I'm writing, and I will not
publish it.

Thanks for your help,
Michael Welter
+1 303 414 4978
mike@introspect.com
```

I once worked on what I thought was the worst Cobol program. Someone
originally tried to write it in Natural (accessing Adabas databases)
and ran out of memory. They then converted the code to Cobol (much the
same) but left the original structure in place. Result 16 coded
accesses of one database file where two would have done the job
adequately. The program was called PRICM03A.

The system was full of PRICs and PINPs. (sorry, bad joke)
```

#### ↳ Re: Looking for the world's most gawd-awful COBOL program

```
Have you tried using the NIST COBOL test suite? These test *ALL* '85 Standard 
COBOL features - and the code is pretty ugly (IMHO).  There are several places 
on the web that will give you information on how to get (and install) these.
```

<a id="t-a9f817f2ad"></a>
## Thread: Wang COBOL alive and well as Wang VS makes a comeback — 42 this month / 49 total

_Topics: [`COBOL's future, legacy, and obsolescence`](topics.md#future)_

### Wang COBOL alive and well as Wang VS makes a comeback

- **From:** tjunker@tjunker.com
- **Date:** 2009-04-14T01:47:35-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bd6e07da-cfb5-4a98-8815-2ed360057fe1@y9g2000yqg.googlegroups.com>`

```
I am happy to report that Wang COBOL is alive and well at numerous
sites around the world.  Although the population of legacy Wang VS
sites is now very small, the new generation of Wang VS, running mostly
on Dell PowerEdge servers under Linux, is growing, with at least 70
sold to date in 11 countries.

The New VS, also called the VS22000 family, is a 100% seamless
virtualization of the Wang VS machine.  It runs the VS Operating
System, all Wang utilities, all language processors, the PACE database
system, and all user applications, all with zero conversion.  It is a
true VS, just freed from proprietary Wang hardware.

It's also the official new generation of the Wang VS, with
participation, support and certification from Compucom (successor to
Getronics North America, which was successor to Wang Global,
originally Wang Laboratories).

The New VS easily supports user populations up to about 500 Lightspeed
PCs or up to about 1,000 using other workstation emulators.  A new VS
OS supporing twice those numbers is in test and should be released
this year.

Performance in the New VS line ranges up to 240% of the legacy high-
end VS18950 released in 1999.  In Wang's "FAST" benchmark system,
that's a FAST rating of about 15,000 versus the VS18950's rating of
6,300.

At any of the performance tiers the New VS performs file I/O much
faster than any legacy VS, resulting in improvements in backups and
file-intensive operations such as reports and massive updates.

The New VS virtualizes not only the VS machine but all the essential
IO Coprocessors (IOCs) as well.  I/O subsystems that now operate
virtually include SCSI, Universal Serial IOC, 802.3, RSF, async and
mag tape.  Physical Wang SCSI disk drives are directly supported as
well as a wider range of physical mag tape technologies than was ever
supported in the legacy line.

WSN (Wang's networking system) now operates over IP networks, as does
RSF (Wang's VS clustering technology).

The New VS even accommodates legacy "928" devices -- legacy
workstations, printers, TCBs and WACS.  It does this by means of a
microcode-loadable PCI Universal Serial IOC and external legacy APAs
for connecting devices by coax, twisted pair or fiber.

Upgrade to the New VS is comparable to upgrade of the system unit in
earlier VS models.  No conversion is required, just restoring or
copying VS volumes into the New VS, configuring, and IPLing.  The
fastest New VS install was the first one sold, in 2005.  It was
installed, accepted and put into production in less than one day.
Most installs take a but longer but involve none of the time, cost or
risks of conversions to other platforms.  No conversion on the planet
to any other platform offers comparable speed and lack of risk.

The VS community has long recognized the Wang VS as the friendliest
and most efficient mainframe class platform to program, administer and
use.  It typically requires far fewer programming and operations staff
than IBM mainframes.

70 New VS systems have been sold in 11 countries and the number
continues to grow.  It has been difficult to contact surviving Wang VS
sites because not even Wang was able to keep track of all the VS
systems in use.  Since Getronics announced end of support for the
legacy VS in 2008 and designated TransVirtual Systems as the exclusive
reseller of Wang VS system software and support, it's now pretty much
of a no-brainer for anyone still using a legacy VS to contact TVS and
move up to the fully viable, reliable, efficient New VS.

The New VS is also a viable platform for those who have never used a
Wang VS but wish to adopt a mainframe-class platform that is more
affordable and much less costly to operate than an IBM mainframe.  It
is a candidate for porting COBOL, RPG II and PL/I applications from
other platforms as well as for development of new applications.

Wang's Professional Application Creation Environment (PACE)is a
powerful 4GL and database useful for rapid development of
applications.  PACE utilizes the native Wang VS transaction processing
features to provide rollback recovery and transaction delineation.
Wang COBOL also supports transaction processing.  PACE supports Host
Language Interface programming in COBOL and RPG II for application
behavior more complex than can be done without programming.
Straightforward PACE apps are created and mained with zero
programming, entirely through its menu-based Data Dictionary and
Application Builder.

There were about 30,000 Wang VS systems extant at its height in the
1980s, in every conceivable industry and in many government
organizations at all levels.  There were Wang VS systems on U.S. Navy
ships and in all U.S. State Department facilities worldwide.  A third
party software catalog last published by Wang in 1989 listed 900 pages
of offerings.

The Wang VS has been tne enterprise system in many companies and
organizations up to and larger than $500 million/year in annual sales
volume and up to several thousands of employees.  It has performed for
concurrent VS user populations up to about 500 Lightspeed users and up
to about 1,000 non-Lightspeed users.  The New VS will soon support
twice those numbers.

The legacy and New VS offers clustering technology that provides for
shared disk volumes, cross-logon, and allocation of Job and Print
classes to nodes in the cluster for automatic routing of submitted
Jobs and printing of Print Jobs.  Up to 16 VS systems can be
clustered.  The VS supports distributed database operations across the
cluster with two-phase commit.

There is also a way to use Wang COBOL natively in Unix (IBM's AIX and
HP's HP/UX).  It is called COBOL ReSource.  It provides a VS-like
environment and supports VS argument passing by reference.  It
contains the VS Procedure Language, COBOL 85 and the essential VS
utilities.  Soft recoverable files are supported, although the PACE
database system is not.

Anyone interested can Google for "Wang VS", "Transvirtual Systems",
"COBOL ReSource", can contact me, or visit the following websites:

www.tjunker.com
www.transvirtualsystems.com
www.lsnvs.com
www.cobolresource.com

Thomas Junker
V.P., TransVirtual Systems
and
proprietor of The Unofficial Wang VS Information Center website
and
agent for COBOL ReSource
```

#### ↳ Re: Wang COBOL alive and well as Wang VS makes a comeback

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-04-14T13:39:20+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gs23m8$bog$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<bd6e07da-cfb5-4a98-8815-2ed360057fe1@y9g2000yqg.googlegroups.com>`

```
In article <bd6e07da-cfb5-4a98-8815-2ed360057fe1@y9g2000yqg.googlegroups.com>,
 <tjunker@tjunker.com> wrote:
>I am happy to report that Wang COBOL is alive and well at numerous
>sites around the world.  Although the population of legacy Wang VS
>sites is now very small, the new generation of Wang VS, running mostly
>on Dell PowerEdge servers under Linux, is growing, with at least 70
>sold to date in 11 countries.

With all appropriate respect due to the stunning growth quoted here, Mr 
Junker - seventy sold in eleven countries! - I must say that I, 
personally, find myself to be moderately underwhelmed.

This is not due to my experience with the WANG as a platform; I found it 
to be a real 'programmer's machine' upon which miracles could be worked in 
an amazingly short amount of time.  Granted that the I-O could get, at 
times, a bit... unstable (file corruption and record loss were, in my 
experience, common in anything more complex than dataset with more than 
two alternate indices); the user community was very supportive and 
miracles (for its time) could be worked.  I generated a COBOL program that 
would, for a given record, generate a pop-up window so that the user could 
jot free-form notes of unlimited length (the 2000-char LRECL took a bit of 
doing to overcome) in 1987.

That was considered to be Quite Something... in 1987.  Sadly, it seemed 
that scarcely a nickel's worth of R&D was invested since then; your 
posting mentioned a list of third-party developers of some 900 pages that 
was printed in 1989.

That was two decades ago.

I wish you the best of luck in your venture.

DD
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Wang COBOL alive and well as Wang VS makes a comeback

- **From:** "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net>
- **Date:** 2009-04-14T13:24:05-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<py4Fl.20748$Qh6.192@newsfe14.iad>`
- **References:** `<bd6e07da-cfb5-4a98-8815-2ed360057fe1@y9g2000yqg.googlegroups.com> <gs23m8$bog$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
What's happening, of course,  is that there are applications developed in
"Wang" that are working well enough that there's no psrticular urge to
replace them, and a dwindling talent pool available to do it in any event.
Prime is another example: I don't think there are any actual Prime machines
still running in western Canada, but I know of a dozen places that are still
running the applications under emulation in Linux environments.  There must
be all sorts of tiny niches like this - I wonder if there are any Singer
apps still running??!?? - and eventually they will all run out of traction
and be abandoned, or will be rewritten for some other environment.  And that
may be a stressful time for the ones responsible.  But facts are facts and
everything has its lifespan.  What's a great pity is that there are concepts
and utilities in each environment that are done exceptionally well (I was
going to say unique but I doubt if anything is actually unique) which will
get lost when the environment is abandoned.  Since I've worked on the Prime
I have been able to implement some of the ideas I came across: not
particularly new but done in ways I hadn't thought of..

I/T seems to lack the practice of other engineering disciplines - that of
preserving history for the benefit of future students of the field.

PL

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:gs23m8$bog$1@reader1.panix.com...

tlmfru wrote:
> What's happening, of course,  is that there are applications
> developed in "Wang" that are working well enough that there's no
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> not particularly new but done in ways I hadn't thought of..

That's a very good point.

I found out many years ago that moving around and getting experience on 
different platforms seems to have a synergistic effect... Your overall 
capability increases to become more than the sum of its parts...:-)

If you stay with one environment it is very easy to think there is only way 
to do things.

>
> I/T seems to lack the practice of other engineering disciplines -
> that of preserving history for the benefit of future students of the
> field.

COBOL code is being carefully preserved and enshrined as we speak. I 
understand billions of lines of it will be around for some time yet...:-)

Whether this is useful to students is debatable. Most of the ones I 
encounter through private mail are simply puzzled by it.

<unreferenced previous snipped>

Pete.

> encounter through private mail are simply puzzled by it.

I fought to the last to keep COBOL at least peripherally in the
program here. (Not as a specific subject, but used in courses
like File Processing.  In the end, I lost out to Java and whatever
will be the next language du jour.  Who kows, maybe after I retire
I will get my wish to once again work with COBOL when they are out
looking for people to maintain/modify all those Information Systems
written in COBOL and still running.  :-)

bill

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Wang COBOL alive and well as Wang VS makes a comeback

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-04-15T13:49:58+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gs4om6$o4o$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<bd6e07da-cfb5-4a98-8815-2ed360057fe1@y9g2000yqg.googlegroups.com> <py4Fl.20748$Qh6.192@newsfe14.iad> <74kml0F13dcc2U1@mid.individual.net> <74m4rnF1454jgU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <74m4rnF1454jgU1@mid.individual.net>,
Bill Gunshannon <billg999@cs.uofs.edu> wrote:
>In article <74kml0F13dcc2U1@mid.individual.net>,
>	"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> writes:

[snip]

>> I found out many years ago that moving around and getting experience on 
>> different platforms seems to have a synergistic effect... Your overall 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>way and looks for specialization rather than generalization.

With all due respect, Mr Gunshannon... 'the industry'?  Now I am reminded 
of... A Story!

I graduated college with a BA in Liberal Arts from what some might call 
the 'liberal-artiest' school in the country; we studied grammar, rhetoric, 
logic, arithmetic, music, geometry and astronomy... and as often as we 
could, from the original texts.  Shortly after that I read an article in 
the Wall Street Journal where the reporter had Important Executive after 
Important Executive saying that what their companies needed were 
generalists, not narrowly-trained specialists; I broke out my trusty 
Olivetti and began sending out cover-sheets and resumes.

I shortly after that began to receive 'ding' notes... 'Thank you for your 
interest in our organisation but at present we do not have any positions 
for which your background is suited'.  I papered an entire wall in my 
bathroom with these so that at appropriate times I could say 'This one's 
for *you*, Kodak... and this one's for *you*, Citibank... don't feel left 
out, DuPont, I've got some left...'

In short... *every* company in *every* industry that I applied to, when 
offered a generalist, said 'thanks, but no'; it wasn't until I picked up 
COBOL - then a language the need for which was frequently seen in 
newspaper-advertisements - that I began to get jobs.

Things now, of course, have changed... and I am the King of England.  I've 
been told that the trend now is to remove human interaction as much as 
possible; a resume is scanned for keywords and if those aren't there then 
consideration is not given.

Who cares if you can wrap your mind around Minkowski's reformulation of 
Einstein's 1905 paper into geometric terms... if you don't know (our 
business need) already then thanks, but no.

As the Germans say, Mr Gunshannon... plus ca change, plus c'est la meme 
chose.

DD
```

>                                                          Now I am reminded 
> of... A Story!
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
> chose.

First, Mr. Gunshannon was by father, now deceased.  My name is "bill".

Now, time for my story.  :-)

I decided almost a decade ago that I was tiring of the academic world
so I gave some thought to what I wanted to do to round out my career.
I decided that of all the things I have done I probably enjoyed writting
COBOL the most.  So, I decided to look for a COBOL position.  Now, over
the past 20-some years I have done COBOL on IBM, Unisys (Sperry in those
days), Prime, RT-11, RSTS/E, VMS, Unix and numerous PC (and other less
popular micros) dialects.  Pretty well rounded background you would think.
All spread out over the period with the VMS being the most current.
But a job search returned only "current CICS experience required" or
"current Unisys experience required".  Of course, VMS is all but dead
and that was my latest.  One would think that with this background it
would demonstrate my ability to work with COBOL in pretty much any
environment.  The best I got was an offer from a very big COBOL shop
in Georgia.  Unfortunately, they were not interested in my COBOL
experience at all and wanted me as a Unix Sys Admin.  Would not even
consider me for one of their many COBOL positions.

But I still hold out hope.  I will retire in under 10 years (hopefully,
not much more than 5) and when that happens, I will hang out my shingle
as a COBOL Programmer available for contract work and see what happens.
Or, just write COBOL for the pure hell of it at home.  :-)

bill
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<74neq7F14kksjU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<bd6e07da-cfb5-4a98-8815-2ed360057fe1@y9g2000yqg.googlegroups.com> <py4Fl.20748$Qh6.192@newsfe14.iad> <74kml0F13dcc2U1@mid.individual.net> <74m4rnF1454jgU1@mid.individual.net> <gs4om6$o4o$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <74m4rnF1454jgU1@mid.individual.net>,
> Bill Gunshannon <billg999@cs.uofs.edu> wrote:
…[34 more quoted lines elided]…
> Citibank... don't feel left out, DuPont, I've got some left...'

ROFL!

Thanks for sharing that, Doc... :-)
>
> In short... *every* company in *every* industry that I applied to,
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> DD

I have to agree. It is sad that it has come to this, but these days 
candidate selection is almost exclusively by software.

I don't normally apply for jobs, but I recently did. It was a top NZ IT job 
and I really would love to have done it. Although I am pretty much coming to 
the end of my "career", I honestly felt I could bring something to this job 
and within a couple of years I could train my replacement and make a real 
difference to the way-less-than-optimum Government service it provides. I 
knocked up a 4 page summary (my full CV is 35 pages and I only produce it at 
interviews, when appropriate; I NEVER give it to pimps...), highlighting the 
best stuff I've been involved with (some of it was specific to the industry 
in question) and stressing what a general all-round capable IT guy I am :-), 
and my enthusiasm for this task. I received a job description (from an 
Agency) and went through it carefully. I had everything they requested, and 
in depth.

I  never even got an interview :-)

I think the CV summary was maybe too general (but my experience is pretty 
general, and I avoid lying in job applications), or perhaps they didn't like 
the photo :-). They said they had received 230 applications, which I found 
quite staggering; I would have expected probably no more than about 50 
people in the country would have been properly qualified to do this job.

I find that what works for me is to sit across a table from someone who 
needs IT support, listen to his/her problem, and suggest possible solution 
outlines, based on a lifetime of IT experience. I don't remember ever 
"failing" an interview. Unfortunately, most people don't get this 
opportunity and are completely at the mercy of inept and often downright 
dishonest pimps.

In my case it doesn't really matter; I applied because I wanted to do the 
job, not because I HAVE to, but it would be too bad if that were not the 
case.

If any of you are looking for jobs I would recommend using Agencies only as 
a SECOND-LEVEL layer. Find out who the IT Directors are at the larger 
companies in your town and send CVs direct. Ideally, try and set up a 
meeting (buy the guy a sandwich or a coffee, keep it relaxed and be 
yourself. Don't be intimidated by someone's corporate standing; respect is 
good, but fear or arse-licking are not... :-))

(I should have really followed this advice myself and gone down to 
Wellington to see their current CEO, but I've just been too busy. Having 
written that, I guess I couldn't have been really serious about getting the 
job... :-))

Pete.
```

- **References:** `<bd6e07da-cfb5-4a98-8815-2ed360057fe1@y9g2000yqg.googlegroups.com> <gs23m8$bog$1@reader1.panix.com> <py4Fl.20748$Qh6.192@newsfe14.iad> <74kml0F13dcc2U1@mid.individual.net>`

```

Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:74kml0F13dcc2U1@mid.individual.net...
> tlmfru wrote:
> >
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>

\

I wasn't thinking of languages, more of concepts.  For instance, the
sequential file update using the "process the lowest key and write record
when the key changes" algorithm should be a standard method (non-OO
vocabulary)  taught to all programmers.  There are any number of "standards"
(in the musical sense, like Flatt & Scruggs) like that which should be
included in curriculi.  As far as the Prime is concerned, the language
(InfoBasic) is tedious, labour-intensive and not worth worrying about (apart
from those sites still using it!)  but the way it implements "select lists "
is definitely worth preserving.

PL
```
