tlmfru wrote:
>
> I/T seems to lack the practice of other engineering disciplines -
that of
> preserving history for the benefit of future students of the field.

You just have to know where to find it. There are substantial efforts
to preserve IT history: the various Computer Museums, bitsavers.org,
textfiles.com, alt.folklore.computers, and so on. Academic work in
computing history is growing, including within the new but popular
field of "software studies".

On this topic, the OP might want to mention the resurrection of Wang
VS on alt.folklore.computers, where such things are frequently
discussed. Preferably with a bit more brevity than the rather lengthy
item here, but that is of course to the author's discretion.
```

<a id="t-6d6cbcfa51"></a>
## Thread: zcobol - first COBOL compiler supporting ANSI/ISO 754 Decimal Floating Point — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers) · [`Migration and conversion`](topics.md#migration)_

### zcobol - first COBOL compiler supporting ANSI/ISO 754 Decimal Floating Point

- **From:** "don@higgins.net" <don@higgins.net>
- **Date:** 2009-04-14T05:28:58-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f37da7d7-75fc-4e01-9327-636a8505097e@e37g2000vbe.googlegroups.com>`

```
zcobol v1.5.00b now supports ANSI/ISO 754 Decimal Floating Point (DFP)
plus ANSI/ISO 754 Binary Floating Point (BFP) .

zcobol supports the new explicit COBOL USAGE types defined in the
draft COBOL 2008 standard: FLOAT-DECIMAL-7, FLOAT-DECIMAL-16, FLOAT-
DECIMAL-34, FLOAT-BINARY-7, FLOAT-BINARY-16, FLOAT-BINARY-34.  In
addition the IBM mainframe default standard Hexadecimal Floating Point
(HFP) is supported using FLOAT-HEX-7, FLOAT-HEX-15, and FLOAT-HEX-30.
The COBOL 2002 standard generic floating point usage types FLOAT-
SHORT, FLOAT-LONG, and FLOAT-EXTENDED default to DFP, but can be
reassigned to any of the 3 types using zcobol option FLOAT(HEX), FLOAT
(BINARY), or the default FLOAT(DECIMAL).  The default for COMP-1 and
COMP-2 in zcobol is HFP for IBM mainframe compatibility.

In addition to the floating point extended data types, zcobol now also
supports the following COMP fields: half word S9(4), full word S9(9),
double word S9(18), and quad word S9(39) and also packed decimal and
zoned decimal up to S9(31).

The zcobol verbs MOVE, ADD, SUBTRACT, MULTIPLY, DIVIDE, IF, and
DISPLAY support the above data types in any combination.  zcobol
generates HLASM IBM mainframe compatible code which can be run at
native speed on IBM z9/z10 mainframes or the code can be compiled,
linked, and executed on any J2SE platform including Windows and Linux
using z390.  The InstallShield download for z390 and zcobol which is
open source is available on www.z390.org.  For more information on
zcobol visit www.zcobol.org.  The download includes demos and new
regression tests for the new data types.  The demo zcobol\demo
\powers.cbl displays powers of 2 up to 126 using the new COMP S9(39)
128 bit integer support.  The 3 regression tests zcobol\test
\TESTHFP1.CBL, TESTBFP1, and TESTDFP1 using FLOAT(HEX), FLOAT(BINARY),
and the default FLOAT(DECIMAL) respectively illustrate the new
floating point code generation and execution capabilities.

Finally you can define a decimal floating point fraction such as 0.1
in COBOL and have it result in exactly 0.1000000 using DFP short
versus 0.09999999 for HFP or BFP short due to irrational result in
base 2 versus base 10.

Don Higgins
Don@higgins.net
Don-Higgins.net
```

---

## Thread: (OT) need off-list help with MS Access (reports) — 2 msgs

### (OT) need off-list help with MS Access (reports)

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-14T16:49:15-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<xx7Fl.152922$UQ6.6748@en-nntp-03.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
I know that there are specific fora for asking questions about this, but I was 
wondering if any of the "usual CLC participants" might be relatively experienced 
using MS Access' report features and might be able to help me with some 
(beginners) questions?  If so, please send me a note off-list.

These are NOT "do your own homework" questions,but they are certainly 
"beginner's" questions that I *should* be able to answer on my own, but just 
haven't been able to (yet).
```

#### ↳ Re: (OT) need off-list help with MS Access (reports)

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-04-15T11:50:20+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<74klpkF148ce3U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<xx7Fl.152922$UQ6.6748@en-nntp-03.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
William M. Klein wrote:
> I know that there are specific fora for asking questions about this,
> but I was wondering if any of the "usual CLC participants" might be
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> but just haven't been able to (yet).

Bill,

I'd strongly recommend that you DON'T use Access Reports for anything except 
the most simple (temporary or interim) reporting, to solve an immediate 
problem, understanding that it will be replaced with a proper solution. 
(Yes, I know that "temporary solutions" often abide for decades; that's 
partly why I wouldn't advocate using it at all...).

The problems I see with it are:

1. It locks you in to Access and is not scalable.
2. It is limited in its capability and requires VBA knowledge if you want to 
do anythig remotely useful with it.

Generally, MS ACCESS works really well as a data repository for small 
(ish)/medium applications with less than 17 workstations sharing it. After 
that you should use SQL Server, or another RDBMS. Access gets in trouble 
when you start using it to develop Access applications for anything but the 
most trivial jobs. As a repository (being accessed by something else) it is 
excellent; as an application (using the built in facilities), not so good.

Many people find Crystal Reports to be an excellent tool... and it works 
against almost ANY RDB. There are demo versions available for free (or there 
used to be... haven't used it for a while and I have a licensed copy of 
version 8); I'm sure you could pick up a second hand copy very cheap.

SQL Server has a reporting capability which I haven't tried, but have heard 
very good things about. (SQL Server Express is free).

Open Source DBs like MySQL and PostgreSQL can use most third party reporting 
tools. I find StimulSoft is outstanding (partly because it integrates 
seamlessly with VS 2008, but mainly because it does provide a true WYSIWYG 
GUI interface where you can simply drag and drop report elements, and it is 
a bit less "complex" than Crystal.) I spent three days playing with 
StimulSoft and was pretty facile with it by the end of that time. It really 
is outstanding.

Having said all of the above, I'll be happy to help you off list.

Pete.
```

---

## Thread: (OT) "honorifics" in CLC — 33 msgs

### (OT) "honorifics" in CLC

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-16T15:46:02-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<fOMFl.36211$oq1.31674@en-nntp-01.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
Just a personal note (and opinion only),

I *still* find it jarring when DD refers to me as "Mr. Klein".  He certainly has 
the right to use that terminology, but to me that way of speaking often (if not 
usually) is used when one wants to SOUND polite while making snide or at least 
derogatory comments about a person - and, as such, is usually used in the 3rd 
person.

Obviously, DD was raised in a different environment than I was but I, for one, 
wouldn't mind at all (and would actually appreciate it) should he ever decide to 
change to using first names (or email aliases) as most other posters in CLC do.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: (OT) "honorifics" in CLC

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-17T12:49:18-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gsae2601rv5@news6.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<ae93e$49e7ad3e$621629c9$26844@ALLTEL.NET>`
- **References:** `<fOMFl.36211$oq1.31674@en-nntp-01.dc1.easynews.com> <VlNFl.58043$0%2.7243@newsfe22.iad> <ae93e$49e7ad3e$621629c9$26844@ALLTEL.NET>`

```
SeaSideSam wrote:
> 
> the use of 'mr', 'mrs', and 'miss' has all to do with 'respect' and
> nothing to do with 'sound'ing like anything.

Since "respect", in this context, is a function of tone, it has
everything to do with "'sound'ing like". Unless you believe there's an
essence of respect that exists outside human interaction (which, on
Usenet, is generally confined to language, and hence tone) - which
would be an interesting metaphysical stance, but I suspect hard to argue.

Further, and more relevantly to Bill's point, a title such as "Mr" -
like any other signifier - cannot by itself determine tone. "Mr" can
certainly be used in a disrespectful manner. Rhetoric is a function of
whole statements (enonces) in their contexts.

And I agree with Bill that the title is often employed in just such a
manner - for a false, mocking veneer of respect over an attack. I
don't believe the Good Doc employs it that way (at least I don't
recall him doing so here); and I don't think Bill was suggesting that
he does. Bill was merely noting that because it is often used that
way, as a reader he finds it jarring.

The author's intention does not determine the reader's interpretation.
(While there are still those, such as Walter Benn Michaels, who argue
that intention *should* determine interpretation, they're in the
minority, and it's a hard claim to support rigorously.)

>  your objections to the use
> of these forms of addressing others is another sign of the times... and
> not a positive sign.

Ah, things were so much better in the Old Days... Hey, you kids, get
off o' my language!

>  i expected better of you mr. klein.

I wasn't aware that Bill was in charge of how other people use the
language.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<13dc6948-ddc6-49b7-a0d4-7e8fb214316c@d2g2000pra.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<fOMFl.36211$oq1.31674@en-nntp-01.dc1.easynews.com> <HV5Gl.62249$TD1.13137@newsfe18.iad> <gsb2t9$r8a$1@reader1.panix.com> <74t4tmF15jo26U1@mid.individual.net> <gscmje$r1u$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
Puzzled... puzzled... puzzled. From where I'm brought out to this
world.

In a debate (courtroom, board meeting, format exhibits), we do address
"Mr." or "Ms." if trying to convey a question or statement. We see it
fits, because we are personally looking at the person we're talking
to. So as not to be rude, we address it so.... however, it connotes
'contradiction' to the other person (sometimes). We also use it in a
formal letter as salutation, in this manner though it connotes
'respect'.

In a chat or group discussions like this.... we address a person by
their "first name" to signify friendship. A buddy gesture is much
welcome in this occasion.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: (OT) "honorifics" in CLC

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-04-20T13:16:55+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gshsk7$7rs$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<fOMFl.36211$oq1.31674@en-nntp-01.dc1.easynews.com> <74t4tmF15jo26U1@mid.individual.net> <gscmje$r1u$1@reader1.panix.com> <13dc6948-ddc6-49b7-a0d4-7e8fb214316c@d2g2000pra.googlegroups.com>`

```
In article <13dc6948-ddc6-49b7-a0d4-7e8fb214316c@d2g2000pra.googlegroups.com>,
Rene_Surop  <infodynamics_ph@yahoo.com> wrote:
>Puzzled... puzzled... puzzled.

Excellent!  Puzzlement can lead to a subject being ignored ('this is too 
confusing... I think I will go re-arrange the contents of my kitchen 
instead') or it can lead to concentration and learning; the latter is one 
of the sub-texts of the Socratic dialogue 'Meno'.  'Learning starts from 
uncertainty or confusion... how can this 'make sense'?'

>From where I'm brought out to this
>world.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>'respect'.

Here, it seems, there are different shadings of words.  As I've said 
before, 'courtesy' - in the sense of 'polished manners' - is something I 
was taught should be granted to all people as a matter of course; 
'courtesy is the grease of the wheels of society' is an aphorism which 
springs to mind.

Respect, on the other hand, is something which I believe must be earned; 
'Mr Jones and I disagree on the best way to approach this problem but from 
what I have seen of the quality of his previous work I will set my own 
methods aside and work with his'... respect is a way in which one allows 
the thoughts/actions/methods of others to be considered superior to one's 
own because... well, because in the past they have shown, despite the 
initial 'this can't be right'... to be... right.  It is a denying of the 
'I know' because the 'I know' of another, while different, has shown that 
it gets things done better or causes one to come to a more attractive 
conclusion.

>
>In a chat or group discussions like this.... we address a person by
>their "first name" to signify friendship. A buddy gesture is much
>welcome in this occasion.

In a group discussion like this courtesy is granted, as a matter of 
course, just as in a walking-down-the-street situation one's first 
reaction is to step back (under non-threatening conditions) and says 
'please, after you'... and that little bit of society rolls a bit more 
smoothly.

But... what do *I* know about proper interactions, I'se jes' a 
COBOL-codin' fool whose manners are so ill-developed that I am best suited 
to talking to machines.

DD
```

<http://groups.google.com/group/comp.lang.cobol/msg/8765013680432240?dmode=source>) 
and were you to have seen me apply this form of address in a manner other 
than uniformly... you might, I'd say, have something.

Unfortunately I extend this courtesy ('unfortunately I extend this 
courtesy?') nigh universally... hence my wry 'nobody gets special 
treatment'... and any more rye and I'd be nigh pumpernickel.

DD
```

Another alternative is that they never really existed the way we
think.   While I have known men who remembered sitting at bus stops to
watch the gals step up into busses showing their calves, it is
possible that media exaggerated their impact due to Hayes code and
other censorships.   I associate the word with hard-boiled detective
novels which had a tough image - but one which was subject to censors.

Censorship has some advantages in art.  In the U.S. it was primarily
sexual censorship, and writers had to come up with nifty dialog that
adults would understand, but which wasn't too obvious.   In the USSR,
the censorship was political, and the writers again had to use their
wits.

achieved). She was leaving an airport at New York and took her suitcases 
out of the exit and put them on the sidewalk. She put her fingers in her 
mouth and gave a loud whistle to summon a taxi.

Up rolled a yellow cab. The gravel-voiced New Yorker took one look at 
her and said, "Lady, with those gams you don't need to whistle !".

Although I couldn't give the exact date: when the "'60's invasion" miniskirt
style began to be less fresh & exciting, and the first tentative showings of
midi-skirts came along, Playboy mag started up an informal group called
GAMS: "Gals/guys against midi-skirts".  It may or may not have had any
effect but the midi was delayed for a couple of years, until suddenly all
the women were wearing those hideous calf-length culottes, which were
succeeded by drab midis: I think this would be about 1977.  Minis didn't
start to reappear until about 1997.

Long live gams!

<a id="t-25f91ac436"></a>
## Thread: SQL wrapper in OO cobol — 9 msgs

_Topics: [`Object-oriented COBOL`](topics.md#oo) · [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases)_

### SQL wrapper in OO cobol

- **From:** Alain Reymond <arwebmail@skynet.be>
- **Date:** 2009-04-23T13:00:10+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<49f04a4a$0$2854$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be>`

```
Would someone be interested in writing (or has someone ever written) an
OO cobol extension to wrap all the embedded sql statements ?

We could have a db class with calls to it with something like

object section.
class-control.
           ooDB is class "oodb".
....
working-storage section.

1 aDb object reference.

procedure division.

invoke ooDb "new" returning aDb
invoke aDb "Connect" using 'dbuser', 'dbpass', 'dbhost', 'dbport', 'dbname'

move 'SELECT * FROM table WHERE foo=1 and bar=2' to query
invoke aDb "Prepare" using query returning ...
invoke aDb "Execute" returning ....

What do you think about it ?

Regards

Alain
```

#### ↳ Re: SQL wrapper in OO cobol

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-04-24T13:14:37+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<75ci3tFp05vpU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<49f04a4a$0$2854$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be>`

```
Alain Reymond wrote:
> Would someone be interested in writing (or has someone ever written)
> an
> OO cobol extension to wrap all the embedded sql statements ?
>

Yes, I have exactly that.

> We could have a db class with calls to it with something like
>
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>

As you have realized, it is necessary to use dynamic SQL. This can have 
serious performance implications so there are other steps necessary to 
counteract this.

Rather than a "DB Class" apply your class to a given set of related tables, 
on a specific database. (Relational DBs are excellent for this... :-))

If you try and do it for each individual table you will have update synch 
problems for related tables, (even using cascaded updates, this gets very 
dodgy very quickly...) and if you try and do it for the whole database, it 
becomes too unwieldy, and the resultant object is too large.

> What do you think about it ?
>
I thought it was a very good idea when I first considered it, about 7 years 
ago. It followed naturally from the VSAM systems we devised in the 60s, 70s, 
and 80s where we used "access modules" that could do every possible action 
against a given dataset, but, of course, none of this was object oriented.

However, using separate modules for data access DID provide a number of 
advantages. In the ensuing years these advantages have been realised and 
formalized and may well have provided the basis for the objects and layers 
that modern software is designed upon.

If you can reduce the functionality to a set of simple operations, you can 
then generate the code easily. The obvious solution is to use a template.

> Regards
>
> Alain

I undertook this initially as a labour of love and largely because everyone 
I spoke to said it couldn't be done. Far too difficult to provide data 
access for all of the infinite ways programmers devise to access data. They 
were nearly right... it has taken me several years to get a solution that 
CAN do everything, but it was worth the effort. (It has also been extremely 
challenging and interesting, with new unforeseen problems popping out of the 
woodwork all the way along...)

I decided to incorporate everything I had learned into a commercial package 
about 3 years ago and it was completed a few months ago. The first customer 
to implement it has volunteered to act as a reference site and is very 
pleased with what they got.

My software generates OO COBOL code for an object that handles a given "set" 
of tables on an RDB. (This is exactly what you proposed above, but instead 
of writing it by hand, I generate it from a toolset written in C#)

The collection of these objects constitutes a Data Access Layer (DAL). A 
"table set" in this context is a set of normalized tables in 2NF. These are 
generated from COBOL COPY books and the OCCURs repeating groups are 
separated out to tables which are linked to the base table in accordance 
with first normal form normalization. All constraints, keys and cascades are 
handled by the toolset.

(I note in passing that if you designed the db table set by hand, the 
Toolset could still generate the maintenance object for it... however, I 
would need to write a module that analysed your DB...)

Separation, even for existing COBOL applications, is automatically generated 
from the COBOL COPY Books. The idea is to break existing integrated, 
monolithic solutions into objects and layers that can be accessed from other 
languages as well as COBOL. The generated objects are currently compiled to 
run in the .NET framework, but they don't HAVE to be. Each object is 
generated from a base template that has all the functions you require to 
maintain data (a Maintenance Template). Each object is actually a COM 
server, so the base templates are referred to as "MOST" (Maintenance Object 
Server Templates). The tool which generates a MOST object to handle a given 
Table Set is called MOSTGEN.

The instantiation, setting of object references, DB connection code, 
analysis and generation of multiple DAL instances for separate threads, 
exception handling, DECLGEN and inclusion of host variables is all 
considered to be MOST "housekeeping"; this is done by another MOST tool. 
There is no need for programmers to have to understand OO in order to use 
MOST; the code is generated and inserted into the existing (or new) 
application code, automatically.

Another tool changes all the existing ISAM references in a program into DAL 
object invokes. The end result is to switch the data source for the 
application from ISAM to RDB, WITHOUT changing the business logic. Data 
access is simply generated into a separate layer of access objects.

 The interface is initially via the existing record layouts and the DAL 
objects take these and apply them to the RDB, or access the RDB and pass a 
populated record layout back to the COBOL application.

However, this is just a starting point. RDB views can also be easily 
incorporated into the DAL so that frequently used elements can have their 
own view and it is not necessary to populate a full COBOL record for them. 
Repeating groups are handled automatically by the DAL and tables embedded in 
the COBOL layout are automatically populated from DAL RDB access. I spent 
many weeks optimizing the dynamic SQL used by the DAL objects and received 
very helpful suggestions and advice from Frederico Fonseca and Robert 
Wagner, which I took on board and incorporated into the final release. 
Combined with the capabability to multithread DAL objects, this ensures that 
performance is excellent. Live tests have been impessive.

The main point is to separate data access from business logic. Applications 
no longer contain SQL or ISAM code. Instead, they invoke equivalent 
functions in an object layer. Performance optimization, changes to data 
requirements, new views of data for specific application code, can all be 
implemented without impact on the business logic. Object encapsulation and 
process isolation ensure that "knock on" errors in maintenance are virtually 
eliminated and regression testing is minimised.

There is not enough space here to describe everything the Toolset does (and 
it is not appropriate for me to promote my own software in this forum...I've 
offered the descriptions here to show that your idea has some very 
interesting ramifications...).

However, I do plan on attaching a Toolset portal to the cobdata web site, 
which will show walkthroughs and demos, along with the actual code 
generated, for people who may be interested in getting their existing COBOL 
into the 21st century.

Generalized classes to handle relational data are definitely viable, but 
there is far more to this than you might think on first inspection.

Objects and layers are where the future lies, small is beautiful...

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: SQL wrapper in OO cobol

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-24T12:29:42-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gst09v5o3k@news1.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<49f04a4a$0$2854$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be>`
- **References:** `<49f04a4a$0$2854$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be>`

```
Alain Reymond wrote:
> Would someone be interested in writing (or has someone ever written) an
> OO cobol extension to wrap all the embedded sql statements ?
> 
> We could have a db class with calls to it with something like...

An alternative is to make database access an aspect of classes that
represent your data abstractions. Thus you might have a Payment class
with a "commit" method that performs the necessary inserts or updates;
a (static) "locate" method that performs the necessary selects and
returns a new Payment object; and so forth.

This lets you use the Implementation pattern within your application
classes, so the application logic doesn't need to know anything about
the database - or even that it's going to a database. RDBMS, indexed
file, whatever; it's transparent to the application.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Parallelism between computer science and experience WAS: Re: SQL wrapper in OO cobol

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-04-25T12:34:37+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<75f44tF183tekU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<49f04a4a$0$2854$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be> <gst09v5o3k@news1.newsguy.com>`

```
Michael Wojcik wrote:
> Alain Reymond wrote:
>> Would someone be interested in writing (or has someone ever written)
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> file, whatever; it's transparent to the application.

Absolutely.

Layers and objects.

It's kind of funny, but back in the days when Computer Science (at least 
commercially) was non-existent, we learned by trying stuff and making it 
better the next time we did it. (You could say it was an empirical, 
iterative approach, but unkinder people would say it was fools stumbling in 
the dark and getting there by trial and error, with a modicum of sheer 
luck....)

Today's programmers are mostly professionals and have the benefits of at 
least some formal training.

In the time I'm speaking of it was normal for bright kids in the business to 
volunteer for the "computer department" (later it became "EDP", then "IT") 
get sent on a two week COBOL course, and come back to start implementing 
business rules. Sometimes they would be aptitude tested, but very often all 
that was required was enthusiasm for the "coming technology", knowledge of 
the business, and ability to learn fast.

Given this, it is hardly unexpected that some very poor computer systems 
were written...(there were some very good ones too, but system development 
was very much a hit and miss affair when judged by today's standards. Formal 
project management was unknown, PERT was still being invented to support the 
Polaris project, and the Waterfall evolved out of IT being burned with 
continually changing requirements.)

"Best practices" evolved for many of us out of experience, and not from 
concepts of programming theory.

But the interesting thing is that very often the practices we embraced were 
the same ones that Acadaemia formalized and taught some time later. (I'm not 
suggesting there weren't papers that had been written, or that NO 
universities were teachng programming and Computer Science; I'm saying that 
formalizing programming was not the common approach on most sites, 
especially COBOL ones, and the majority of COBOL programmers at the time had 
no formal programming training, other than that provided by courses run by 
their vendor.)

The idea of separation and patterns WAS being implemented on commercial 
sites but the people doing it did not realise that that was what they were 
doing.  I mentioned in another post in this thread that we built access 
modules for large VSAM-based systems. In fact, I remember leading a team in 
the mid-1970s that built a pan-European database for a major international 
corporation in England. It was entirely VSAM based and templated access 
modules were used. (It was so effective that the users and the management 
resisted violently some years later, when it was decided that the company 
should move to IMS.) We decided to use separation between the the database 
and the applications because the system had to work in 14 countries and some 
of their requirements were specific to that country. We wanted generalised 
business logic with hooks for country specific functions and we felt that if 
everything was accessing the database from everywhere, it would just be a 
jungle.

Because of the architecture we chose, it was not a major trauma to replace 
the VSAM base with IMS, and later this was replaced with DB2.

Similar experience was applied with user interfaces; it really didn't matter 
where the data came from (punched cards, IMS/DC, or CICS BMS) as long as the 
business function received a buffer with data in it that was meaningful for 
that process.

By the mid-80s PCs had arrived, but the concepts of networking were still 
new to many of us. We were used to a centralized processor driving 
everything and the idea of having different "cores" working asynchronously 
was foreign. (In fact, to some, it was anathema...)

I remember the sense of wonder I experienced when I first realised that 
suitably wrapped components could run ANYWHERE on the network, and didn't 
need to be queued up waiting for a processor... With the advent of the 
Internet it meant that encapsulated functionality could be flashed around 
the world and run wherever it was needed. A long way from COBOL on a 360-30, 
driving a 2260 in Foreground 1, in 16K..:-)

For many years now (since I first got into object oriented programming, in 
fact)  I have been sold on the idea of separation and encapsulation. The 
more systems are tiered and encapsulated, the lower the risk of change 
becomes. Smaller granularity presents a smaller risk profile when 
maintenance occurs.

A few years back I came across some formal stuff on patterns (the kind of 
thing that is taught today in most college CS courses). It made me smile 
when I realised that much of this was simply telling me in academic language 
what I already knew. But I'm glad this distilled experience is now available 
to the rising generation.

Today's programmers need to know much more than we did in the early days, 
and it is good to see that need is being filled by formal training.

Computer systems are observably getting better, in general. We trust our 
lives to them daily when we fly or drive or undergo surgery.

Web based systems are providing functionality wherever it is needed, not 
just within the store, but around the world.

A whole generation of "computer literate" people who are not intimidated by 
the technology and expect more from systems than we ever did, are arriving 
or in the pipeline. Technologies we could not have dreamed of 40 years ago 
are emerging and being eagerly embraced.

Programming technology is evolving to support it.

And the key ingredients in this evolving technology are objects and layers.

Pete.
```

Michael Wojcik a ï¿½crit :

You are absolutely right. But I was focusing on a generic wrapper to

You are absolutely right. But I was focusing on a generic wrapper to access the database. 
Using ESQL and Dynamic SQL, we should be able to write a wrapper with the following
class and methods :
Encapsulates a database file:
class DB
    connect
    close
    execQuery
    getTable
    lastRowId

Encapsulates a db query result set:
class DBQuery
    numFields 
    fieldName
    fieldType
    fieldValue
    getIntField
    getFloatField
    getStringField
    fieldIsNull
    eof
    nextRow
    finalize

a method to obtain a complete table's contents:
class DBTable
    DBTable
    numFields
    numRows
    fieldName
    fieldValue
    getIntField
    getFloatField
    getStringField
    fieldIsNull
    setRow

Just an idea...

Regards

Alain Reymond

nag  <visitnag@gmail.com> wrote:
>We are using sf cobol... when we are executing a program on some
>data... it gave the following error...
>
>"Segmentation fault...." what must be the reason for this error?

From http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Segmentation_fault :

--begin quoted text:

A segmentation fault occurs when a program attempts to access a memory 
location that it is not allowed to access, or attempts to access a memory 
location in a way that is not allowed (for example, attempting to write to 
a read-only location, or to overwrite part of the operating system).

--end quoted text

>Our
>data file is a flat file which contain numbers and alfa....

There are a lot of data files like that, or so I've seen.

DD
```

#### ↳ Re: segmentation fault.....

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-24T12:33:19-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gst0a06o3k@news1.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<3e680bb0-41e9-4eec-8b9e-812cd68ce133@w31g2000prd.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<3e680bb0-41e9-4eec-8b9e-812cd68ce133@w31g2000prd.googlegroups.com>`

```
nag wrote:
> We are using sf cobol... when we are executing a program on some
> data... it gave the following error...
> 
> "Segmentation fault...." what must be the reason for this error?

There's a bug in your program, or in something that it calls. An
unhandled illegal memory access is always a bug (unless you're testing
what happens when a memory access violation isn't handled). For that
matter, a handled illegal memory access is often a bug.

There is a considerable collection of literature on how to diagnose
segmentation violations on Unix (which I presume you're using, since
most other popular OSes use different terminology for illegal memory
access). I suggest consulting it.
```

verify the integrity of incoming data might assist in minimising such 
difficulties in the future.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: segmentation fault.....

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-26T13:44:25-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<fY1Jl.63766$641.34331@en-nntp-10.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<3e680bb0-41e9-4eec-8b9e-812cd68ce133@w31g2000prd.googlegroups.com> <defb9a89-f232-4e1c-a969-17d6c4cf1e59@y34g2000prb.googlegroups.com> <01d06019-54de-4a8c-874c-bcaa053e5c29@x1g2000prh.googlegroups.com>`

```
If you are seeing "funny" (unexpected) characters ONLY as the last character if 
a field, then you may want to tell us how you are "looking at" the data.  Often, 
a single byte of data will contain both a numeric digit AND a nibble of data 
indicating the sign of the entire field.  When looked at by SOME external 
(non-programming) methods, such bytes may be displayed as "funny characters".

Such fields WILL pass an "IF NUMERIC" test - and should be processed correctly.

NOTE WELL:
  I don't know of any time that "bad data" causes a "segmentation fault" - with 
the possible (but unlikely) case of where the "bad data" is in the object of an 
OCCURS DEPENDING ON clause *or* when it is used in an ALTER ... DEPENDING ON 
clause.  The former usually causes different symptoms and the latter is so 
unusual that I can't even guess what symptom it would cause.
```

## Thread: Funny characters — 8 msgs

### Funny characters

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-25T16:24:25-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

I have to modify a program written in PowerCOBOL to accept Cyrillic 
characters.

The selected font is Tahoma which admits of Cyrillic letters.

Any up-front tips before I start digging?

Thanks.

#### ↳ Re: Funny characters

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-25T17:23:01-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a3MIl.204400$Yx2.4456@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
Are you talking about Cyrillic characters in source code (e.g. user-defined 
words) - or in literals - or in data? Each of these has (slightly) different 
issues.

"font" issues shouldn't really be an issue - except in outputting reports.
```

#### ↳ Re: Funny characters

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-04-26T10:59:02+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<75hitoF18a7g6U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<AMqdnSO1vIAR4m7UnZ2dnUVZ_qmdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
HeyBub wrote:
> I have to modify a program written in PowerCOBOL to accept Cyrillic
> characters.

Having accepted them, presumably you must then process them? This sounds 
interesting...:-)

>
> The selected font is Tahoma which admits of Cyrillic letters.
>
> Any up-front tips before I start digging?

Yes.

1. Change the font property on Text Box controls which will be accepting 
Cyrillic, to be Tahoma.

You said "accept Cyrillic" but if you need to display Cyrillic as well...

2.  For displayed Message boxes ensure that there are no hard coded literal 
displays. (Always display a field in memory that has been pre-processed to 
contain Cyrillic)

It will be difficult to manipulate Cyrillic characters internally unless you 
switch to a Cyrillic code page. If you have mixed input where it is 
sometimes Cyrillic and sometimes something else, you may need to detect what 
was entered and switch code pages accordingly. This sounds challenging to 
me.

Let us know how you solve it.

I take it there is no available function that can "translate" between 
Cyrillic and normal text?

Pete.
```

#### ↳ SOLVED: Funny characters

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-28T09:45:55-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<hIadnSHfMc0-i2rUnZ2dnUVZ_t6dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<AMqdnSO1vIAR4m7UnZ2dnUVZ_qmdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
HeyBub wrote:
> I have to modify a program written in PowerCOBOL to accept Cyrillic
> characters.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Turns out it was the program that was the problem, not the font.

I suppose one can be TOO careful...

After accepting input data the program checked each character for being in 
the range " " to "~", thereby preventing the user (or the user's cat) from 
inputting invalid characters.

viz.:

    IF INPUT-STRING(I:1) < " " OR > "~"
        MOVE SPACE TO INPUT-STRING(I:1)

Actually, the test is still there unless the installation specifies they 
will be using a non-western font. If so, the user will just have to be 
careful and consign the felines to the hall.

The purpose of this drill is to allow a new customer some degree of local 
compatibility. The new customer is in Azerbaijan (!).

Thanks for thinking about the issue.
```

<a id="t-a2c9039b19"></a>
## Thread: ORACLE takes over Sun (rest of us may have to live in darkness...) — 10 this month / 12 total

_Topics: [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases) · [`Web, XML, modern integration`](topics.md#web)_

### ORACLE takes over Sun (rest of us may have to live in darkness...)

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-04-27T23:48:33+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<75lkciFtcsqpU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In case you haven't heard: http://www.sun.com/third-party/global/oracle/

I'd be interested to hear any comments or thoughts you may have about this.

Here are mine...

1. This is a really significant event for IT. The President of ORACLE thinks 
it is a better deal than Oracle acquiring Siebel, PeopleSoft and BEA. I 
think it will be good for Java, although it may not remain a free language 
much longer.

2. In the same way that MicroSoft pushes SQL Server with C# and .NET, ORACLE 
will be pushing ORACLE (DB) and Java (when they are not pushing their ERP 
offerings...). It is a powerful combination.

3. Most of the SAP systems I have worked with have used ORACLE (DB) as their 
RDB. Yet the two companies are bitter rivals when it comes to ERP systems. 
Could SAP be the next acquisition on ORACLE's list? Between the two of them, 
they have pretty much carved up the ERP package solutions market. It is very 
arguable who is winning or is likely to...

Assuming a clear leader emerges, what does that mean for the rest of us? Are 
corporates likely to continue software development when they can outsource 
the whole IT deal to an ERP package that does everything? (It is fair to 
assume that the resource backing of whichever company wins will be so huge 
that the end ERP product will be pretty close to definitive. Both companies 
are looking to the Web for the future: ORACLE with Fusion and SAP with 
NetWeaver. Early days yet, but both solutions look promising...)

4. What about COBOL? If history is anything to go by, ORACLE is not a "COBOL 
friendly" company. When they acquired PeopleSoft, which was originally 
written completely in COBOL, they moved to replace it as quickly (and 
economically) as possible. While there are still COBOL functions in 
PeopleSoft to this day, the preferred solution is to use "PeopleCode" for 
application development. This is more C-like and (of course) is object 
oriented.  It is unlikely that any of the offerings are going to use COBOL, 
especially as they now "own" Java...

The single most important thing about this, in my opinion, is that, within a 
given IT market, it is a bit like Highlander ("There can be only one..."). 
Anything that poses a market threat to the leader gets acquired, or forced 
out of business. (It is easier than competing, in the long run requires less 
resource, and provides better long term ROI... (ORACLE say that Sun will 
provide  a $1.5 billion revenue stream in the first year... more than that 
in the second year...ORACLE is currently worth around $23 billion (per 
year... largest ERP software company in the world), SAP around $14 billion 
per year...)... it would seem to be a no-brainer to take over SAP...

One fly in the ERP ointment is the effect of the yet-to-be-understood 
"Cloud". If major corporations go for SAAS and decide not to buy software 
solutions, just to rent services which can then be spooned onto their own 
corporate repositories (so there are no concerns about physical corporate 
data security) and distributed to wherever needed, by the local intranet or 
VPN, this could have a serious effect on both the ERP contenders.

One company that is an emerging player in this game is called NetSuite. They 
are currently looking to get into the big league of cloud computing and 
could present a tangible threat to both ORACLE and SAP if the SAAS model 
really catches on.

Guess who owns it? (52%) [OK, I'll save you looking it up...Larry Ellison 
(and family), the CEO of ORACLE.] :-)

It makes me wonder what is likely to happen in the COBOL arena... How long 
will vendors continue scratching to sell expensive compilers and 
maintenance, and who will be the ONE? As the user base evaporates, 
competition becomes fiercer. Open COBOL is free and is looking like a very 
viable alternative. (There are good reports from a company in Texas that is 
now running their production systems in Open COBOL, having migrated their 
previous COBOL without too much difficulty.)

It seems to me that Micro Focus and IBM have little choice really; neither 
of them can drop their existing COBOL user base, so they have to provide an 
alternative pathway to the future. (Although MF are talking up continuing 
COBOL viability, the words ring hollow, and the world has largely voted with 
its feet. Cancelling the show in Dallas hasn't helped credibility, even 
though the online idea is a good one. Like IBM, they are trapped in a 
situation where they realise that customers want to be net based, but they 
can't quite bring themselves to tell customers that once they ARE net based, 
COBOL is no longer a good option. Many customers will use the available 
tools to "migrate" to the network and then find that the old approaches are 
just not workable. Yes, their systems are now running in networked COBOL, 
but they fart like dogs and require new technology to stop doing so... 
Objects and layers are foreign concepts to most procedural COBOL 
programmers. No attempt that I can see is being made to educate or retrain 
anybody. It's OK, we just recompile for the new platform and everything will 
be fine...

(I see a glimmer of hope as new, younger, programmers who have some formal 
training and exposure to other languages arrive; they are unlikely to settle 
for procedural code on the network.)

Fujitsu (Japan) seem to have sold off their COBOL interest (or maybe they 
just franchised it), at least as far as outside-Japan is concerned... (Not 
sure what is happening with Fujitsu-owned Siemens... procedural COBOL seems 
to be alive and well in Germany :-))

On the basis of what I see and hear, it seems to me that the next few years 
will generally not be a good time to be dependent on COBOL or locked in to a 
COBOL vendor.

The uncertainty surrounding it poses a level of risk that many will find too 
high.

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: ORACLE takes over Sun (rest of us may have to live in darkness...)

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-28T12:07:05-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gt7b4r02mu3@news1.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<EsyJl.13763$941.2101@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`
- **References:** `<75lkciFtcsqpU1@mid.individual.net> <EsyJl.13763$941.2101@bgtnsc04-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```
Arnold Trembley wrote:
> 
> IBM is a big supporter of Java, and it will be interesting to see if
> Java remains free.

IBM is the biggest Java promoter, and has been for some time. There's
no question about Java remaining free - it's a published standard,
with many implementations. Oracle has no means to suppress free Java.

To be honest, I don't see how the question even comes up. Java wasn't
actually controlled by Sun when Oracle bought them. JSE, JME, and
Glassfish were all released by Sun under GPLv2 in November 2006. Sun's
OpenJDK release included the HotSpot JVM as well as the JDK.

(Sun's version of the GPL includes their special "Classpath
exception", but all that does is permit Java applications built with
GPL'd Java tools and classes to be used with non-GPL classes. It
bypasses the "viral" aspect of the GPL, in other words. And that's a
Good Thing, for everyone who isn't a FLOSS zealot.)

Oracle might have control of the JCP, but that's really only a concern
 for people obsessed with FLOSS purity. The JCP is relevant only as
long as it remains open. If Oracle tried to close it down, Java
developers and implementation providers would just start ignoring it.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: ORACLE takes over Sun (rest of us may have to live in darkness...)

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-04-30T15:47:58+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<75slbdF19ljj6U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<75lkciFtcsqpU1@mid.individual.net> <770d2f29-05cd-45d0-a1c3-6c6f583b295d@g31g2000pra.googlegroups.com>`

```
Rene_Surop wrote:
>> 2. In the same way that MicroSoft pushes SQL Server with C# and
>> .NET, ORACLE will be pushing ORACLE (DB) and Java (when they are not
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> eaten the COBOL market for sure.

Hmmm... not quite :-)

COBOL will be with us for a little while yet, but it will be a "legacy 
language" that people will be migrating away from.

Finding the best ways to do this so that a HUGE existing investment does not 
need to be written off is one of the challenges such sites are facing.

I have placed a good deal of material about this on the newly revamped 
COBDATA Website, which will be released next Monday.

>
>>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> we're talking of "our" group comp.lang.cobol

Have you noticed how quiet this group has become?

I think there is a certain amount of worry, confusion and general 
bewilderment. (Or maybe everybody is now so expert in COBOL there is no need 
for further discussion or help? :-)  Personally, I come here for the 
off-topic discussions as much as for the on-topic ones. The stimulation of 
bright people discussing the world in general is very appealing to me. Like 
I have always said, I'll be here as long as interacting here is fun.

Where do we go from here?

I'm hoping to address this and give at least one man's view, on the COBDATA 
site. Oddly enough I am optimistic about a future for COBOL code, but there 
are a few provisos. It is NOT going to be the development language of the 
future, but there is still a useful place for it. If the existing codebase 
can be leveraged (and, who better to do this than COBOL programmers?), then 
much of what is there can be salvaged and improved. Existing programmers may 
need to upskill in order to do this, but that is no bad thing. Programmers, 
like most species of "odd" organism, only face extinction if they refuse to 
adapt.

All of us need to accept that COBOL is not and will not be, the "King" it 
once was, but that doesn't mean it is worthless. Talk to your colleagues and 
managers. Point out that throwing out or redeveloping the existing COBOL 
codebase is an expensive and unnecessary exercise. IT CAN be salvaged and it 
is generally worth salvaging. Applications that have run successfully for 
years have to be worth something. Modernization does not mean everything has 
to be rewritten in Java. :-) ERP solutions are getting better and better, 
but they aren't getting cheaper and cheaper. A very good case can be made 
for salvaging and modernizing the COBOL codebase, rather than replacing 
everything with an ERP solution.

BUT, having modernized and salvaged what we can, it then has to be possible 
to use it with the new technology.  Can existing COBOL run with C# or Java? 
Can it be embedded in a Web page or run on a Web Server? Can COBOL 
functionality be utilized by modern scripting languages like PHP, Python, 
and Ruby? Can we use our salvaged, recycled functions with new desktop 
applications? Can our legacy code be easily embedded into Web Services and 
run as part of an SOA? What about a hybrid of ERP with callouts to COBOL 
functionality? Can COBOL handle this?

The answer to all of the above is a resounding "YES".

I'm hoping that people who frequent this forum will post their comments and 
ideas here after visiting the COBDATA site. On the site I have tried to 
present a new view for COBOL.

>
>>
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> with only C# IDE dev. In IT, changes are inevitable.

I understand what you are saying, Rene. It is insightful.

>
> BUT, then again... players like MF and IBM still have their move in a
> chess game.

I s'pose it IS a bit like chess...

This year I resolved to play chess better (I am absolutely crap at it...) 
and I play at least 3 games every day against my computer. For many weeks it 
was a saddening experience as I got thrashed every day. I set it to "novice" 
and it STILL beat the pants off me. But, ever so slowly, I began to see 
things I had not noticed before. I was able to nip devastating knight 
attacks in the bud, learned to protect my flanks, came to respect my 
opponent and realise that if it had apparently made a mistake, it was a 
"mistake" I would pay for dearly a few moves down the track... At last I 
beat it and then managed to do so about 50% of the time. I moved to 
"intermediate" and it immmediately annihilated me with merciless regularity 
again. Game after game, I got trashed. Then I started to win occasionally. 
(I honestly thought the program was letting me win so I wouldn't quit playng 
with it. Later, I realised this wasn't the case; it simply wasn't performing 
the full searches it would have if the level had been higher. I currently 
win or draw about 20% of the games I play. The computer is playing to a 
depth of 4 moves ahead (it's maximum is 9; Grand Master is 8), so I still 
have much to learn, but I can see I've made progress...

Using your analogy, neither MF nor IBM are the Fischers or Spasskys of the 
modern IT tournament, but they are both important players.

Pete.
```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:75slbdF19ljj6U1@mid.individual.net...
> COBOL will be with us for a little while yet, but it will be a "legacy 
> language" that people will be migrating away from.
>
> Finding the best ways to do this so that a HUGE existing investment does 
> not need to be written off is one of the challenges such sites are facing.

I'm not so sure "written off" would be the best choice of words to describe 
the disposition of that "huge initial investment"  in COBOL-based 
applications.

After all, COBOL-based application have served millions of users for over 
fifty years - and continue to do so.

I'd say overall, COBOL-based applications have provided a pretty decent 
return on the investment; they have paid their own way and then some.

Kind of like the 1996 Mercury Grand Marquis I bought new. It's no longer in 
the best of health, having suffered the ravages of thirteen Wisconsin 
winters spending all those cold and lonely nights sleeping alone outdoors, 
but I really cannot complain I have not got my money's worth.  (And only 
101,000 miles on it! ).


MCM
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: ORACLE takes over Sun (rest of us may have to live in darkness...)

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-04-30T13:25:12+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gtc8ro$365$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<75lkciFtcsqpU1@mid.individual.net> <75slbdF19ljj6U1@mid.individual.net> <OBgKl.15476$hc1.4551@flpi150.ffdc.sbc.com> <75tlj5F1a574vU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <75tlj5F1a574vU1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>Michael Mattias wrote:

[snip]

>> Kind of like the 1996 Mercury Grand Marquis I bought new. It's no
>> longer in the best of health, having suffered the ravages of thirteen
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>money to a deposit on a newer car, right?

Bear in mind, Mr Dashwood, that in speaking with Mr Mattias about his 
vehicle you are, as some say, 'talkin' to a 'nother guy 'bout his car'; 
the processes involved in such an interaction are not, in my experience, 
the same as Corporate Discussions about how large-scale computer systems 
are to be dealt with.

With a car, for me, it is simple: when expenses for repairs (outside of 
regular maintenance such as tires, oil, brakes, the usual wear-and-tear of 
Automotive Life) over a six-month period average out to the monthly 
payment for a new vehicle... then it is time to grab a fresh buggy, 
period, and not die the Death of a Thousand Visits to the Mechanic.

In the Corporate World - based on my experience, admittedly a worm's-eye 
view - any situation which requires spending money is met, almost 
reflexively, with 'That costs too much... can't you do something else with 
what we already have?

Never mind that the system, by any set of measures, has 'paid for itsself' 
decades ago, never mind that the system, by accounting practises, was 
fully depreciated as a business expense when Edward VII was still on the 
throne, never mind that a change will allow for easier incorporation of 
new features and technologies which will be to the company's strategic 
advantage *and* that the costs involved generate certain tax advantages... 
the reflexive response is 'That costs too much... can't you do something 
else with what we already have?'

DD
```

#### ↳ Re: fxd to html

- **From:** Alain Reymond <arwebmail@skynet.be>
- **Date:** 2009-04-30T17:58:42+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<49f9cac7$0$2868$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9e1c91b4-876b-4668-b963-eed06f4251f1@n7g2000prc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<9e1c91b4-876b-4668-b963-eed06f4251f1@n7g2000prc.googlegroups.com>`

```
mario a ï¿½crit :
> hi everyone,
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>   
Robodoc is what you need!

It is a simple yet powerful documentation system.
http://www.xs4all.nl/~rfsber/Robo/robodoc.html

You include your documentation in the source code. Then, you generate an
html file with the complete doc.
It is easy, freeware and useful.

Here is an example of the comments placed at the beginning og a source
file :

       *>***h* Application/myprogram
       *> NAME
       *>  myprogram
       *> FUNCTION
       *>  Description of what the programm does
       *>  and so on ...
       *> USAGE
       *>  Is called by
       *> INPUTS
       *>  lnk-i1-duplicata : 1 if true
       *>                     0 if false
       *> OUTPUT
       *>  A PostScript file or pdf file
       *> USES
       *>  prog1
       *>  prog2
       *>  prog3
       *> USED BY
       *>  prog4
       *>  prog5
       *> HISTORY
       *>  23/07/2008 : modification 2
       *>  09/10/2007 : modification 1
       *>  08/06/2006 : creation
       *>**


If you do the same with prog1, prog2, etc..., Robodoc will generate the
links.

Robodoc does not come with a definition for Cobol. You can easily use it
by creating a cobol.rc file that contains the definitions for cobol.
Here is what I use :
#
# Cobol.rc
#
accept files:
    *.cbl
headertypes:
    p  Paragraph
header markers:
    *>***
remark markers:
    *>
end markers:
    *>**
options:
    --src ./.
    --doc api
    --singledoc
    --html
    --tell
    --nodesc
    --index
    --sections
    --toc

Then, call robodoc so :
    robodoc --rc  cobol.rc

Hope this helps.

Regards,

Alain
```

## Thread: Questionnaire for academic research on the programming language COBOL — 1 this month / 3 total

### Questionnaire for academic research on the programming language COBOL

- **From:** Anthony Aquino <anthonyjefferson@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2009-04-30T11:08:29-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5196fa60-0317-493d-8781-9ad10a3c515d@r31g2000prh.googlegroups.com>`

```

Hi guys, how are you?

Well, like I said weeks ago, I was preparing a questionnaire about
COBOL. Now its ready.

If you have some minutes to answer, can you answer the questions,
please ?
I will post the statistics here in the group soon, when I finish my
monograph. Maybe some of you will be interested.
The last day for answering the questionnaire is monday (may, 4).

If you want, you can spread the questionnaire link, no problem, more
answers, more accuracy.

Guidelines:
• The purpose of the questionnaire is to assess the COBOL programming
language by means of the criteria for Robert W. Sebesta.
• This questionnaire is designed to programmers (COBOL or not).
• The questionnaire contains questions that follow on COBOL, which
must be scored on a scale ranging from 1 (low, poor, poor) to 6 (high,
better, stronger).
• Some questions provide examples of code that has to evaluate a
particular feature of language.

My english it not that great, but I tried my best when making a good
translation from the portuguese version (I'm brazilian and COBOL is
very used here), so sorry for any typos.

Questionnaire link:
https://spreadsheets.google.com/viewform?hl=pt_BR&formkey=cnlySF9pbEVQbGpiTnZzZFJ6azh1b3c6MA

Thank you,
Anthony.
```
